<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the internal organ. <p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p>The <b>heart</b> is a muscular <a href="/wiki/Organ_(biology)" title="Organ (biology)">organ</a> in most <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>. This organ pumps <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabers_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-tabers-7">[1]</a></sup> The pumped blood carries <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> to the body, while carrying <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste" title="Metabolic waste">metabolic waste</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011157_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011157-8">[2]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>, the heart is approximately the size of a closed <a href="/wiki/Fist" title="Fist">fist</a> and is located between the lungs, in the <a href="/wiki/Mediastinum" title="Mediastinum">middle compartment</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">chest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore's_6_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore's_6-9">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>In humans, other mammals, and birds, the heart is divided into four chambers: upper left and right <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">atria</a> and lower left and right <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StarrEvers2009_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-StarrEvers2009-10">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K2008_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-K2008-11">[5]</a></sup> Commonly the right atrium and ventricle are referred together as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_heart" title="Right heart">right heart</a> and their left counterparts as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_heart" title="Left heart">left heart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960-12">[6]</a></sup> Fish, in contrast, have two chambers, an atrium and a ventricle, while most reptiles have three chambers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K2008_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-K2008-11">[5]</a></sup> In a healthy heart blood flows one way through the heart due to <a href="/wiki/Heart_valve" title="Heart valve">heart valves</a>, which prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac_regurgitation" title="Cardiac regurgitation">backflow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore's_6_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore's_6-9">[3]</a></sup> The heart is enclosed in a protective sac, the <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardium</a>, which also contains a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Pericardial_fluid" title="Pericardial fluid">fluid</a>. The wall of the heart is made up of three layers: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicardium" title="Epicardium">epicardium</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myocardium" title="Myocardium">myocardium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Endocardium" title="Endocardium">endocardium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The heart pumps blood with a rhythm determined by a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacemaker_cells" title="Pacemaker cells">pacemaker cells</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sinoatrial_node" title="Sinoatrial node">sinoatrial node</a>. These generate a current that causes the heart to contract, traveling through the <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_node" title="Atrioventricular node">atrioventricular node</a> and along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conduction_system_of_the_heart" title="Conduction system of the heart">conduction system of the heart</a>. In humans, deoxygenated blood enters the heart through the right atrium from the <a href="/wiki/Superior_vena_cava" title="Superior vena cava">superior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior venae cavae</a> and passes it to the right ventricle. From here it is pumped into <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, where it receives oxygen and gives off carbon dioxide. Oxygenated blood then returns to the left atrium, passes through the left ventricle and is pumped out through the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systemic_circulation" title="Systemic circulation">systemic circulation</a>, traveling through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arteries" title="Arteries">arteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arteriole" title="Arteriole">arterioles</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capillaries" title="Capillaries">capillaries</a>—where <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> and other substances are exchanged between blood vessels and cells, losing oxygen and gaining carbon dioxide—before being returned to the heart through <a href="/wiki/Venule" title="Venule">venules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011101,_157–158,_180_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011101,_157–158,_180-14">[8]</a></sup> The heart beats at a resting <a href="/wiki/Heart_rate" title="Heart rate">rate</a> close to 72 beats per minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011105–107_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011105–107-15">[9]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">Exercise</a> temporarily increases the rate, but lowers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resting_heart_rate" title="Resting heart rate">resting heart rate</a> in the long term, and is good for heart health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall20111039–1041_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall20111039–1041-16">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">Cardiovascular diseases</a> (CVD) are the most common cause of death globally as of 2008, accounting for 30% of deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_CVD_2013_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_CVD_2013-17">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrisons_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisons-18">[12]</a></sup> Of these more than three-quarters are a result of <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">coronary artery disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_CVD_2013_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_CVD_2013-17">[11]</a></sup> Risk factors include: <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">smoking</a>, being <a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">overweight</a>, little exercise, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_cholesterol" title="High cholesterol">high cholesterol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_blood_pressure" title="High blood pressure">high blood pressure</a>, and poorly controlled <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[13]</a></sup> Cardiovascular diseases frequently do not have symptoms or may cause <a href="/wiki/Chest_pain" title="Chest pain">chest pain</a> or <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a>. Diagnosis of heart disease is often done by the taking of a <a href="/wiki/Medical_history" title="Medical history">medical history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">listening</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart-sound" title="Heart-sound">heart-sounds</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Stethoscope" title="Stethoscope">stethoscope</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrocardiogram" title="Electrocardiogram">ECG</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echocardiogram" title="Echocardiogram">echocardiogram</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore's_6_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moore's_6-9">[3]</a></sup> Specialists who focus on diseases of the heart are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiologists" title="Cardiologists">cardiologists</a>, although many specialties of medicine may be involved in treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrisons_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisons-18">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anatomy_of_the_human_heart" title="Anatomy of the human heart">Anatomy of the human heart</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_and_shape">Location and shape</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="16" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv" height="220" id="mwe_player_2" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv/220px--Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="818502" data-framerate="20" data-height="384" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file, 384 × 384 (819 kbps)" data-width="384" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv" type='video/ogg; codecs="theora"'/><source data-bandwidth="513768" data-framerate="20" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/dd/Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv/Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Real-time_MRI_-_Thorax.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Real-time_MRI" title="Real-time MRI">Real-time MRI</a> of the human heart</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heart_near.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Heart_near.png/170px-Heart_near.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Heart_near.png/255px-Heart_near.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Heart_near.png/340px-Heart_near.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heart_near.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The human heart is in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">thorax</a>, with its apex pointing to the left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[14]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The human heart is situated in the <a href="/wiki/Mediastinum" title="Mediastinum">mediastinum</a>, at the level of <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_vertebrae" title="Thoracic vertebrae">thoracic vertebrae</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_thoracic_vertebra" title="Fifth thoracic vertebra">T5</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eighth_thoracic_vertebra" title="Eighth thoracic vertebra">T8</a>. A double-membraned sac called the <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardium</a> surrounds the heart and attaches to the mediastinum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[15]</a></sup> The back surface of the heart lies near the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a>, and the front surface known as the sternocostal surface sits behind the <a href="/wiki/Sternum" title="Sternum">sternum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Costal_cartilage" title="Costal cartilage">rib cartilages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The upper part of the heart is the attachment point for several large blood vessels—the <a href="/wiki/Venae_cavae" title="Venae cavae">venae cavae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_trunk" title="Pulmonary trunk">pulmonary trunk</a>. The upper part of the heart is located at the level of the third costal cartilage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The lower tip of the heart, the <b>apex</b>, lies to the left of the sternum (8 to 9 cm from the <a href="/wiki/Midsternal_line" title="Midsternal line">midsternal line</a>) between the junction of the fourth and fifth ribs near their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Articulation_(anatomy)" title="Articulation (anatomy)">articulation</a> with the costal cartilages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest part of the heart is usually slightly offset to the left side of the chest (though occasionally it may be <a href="/wiki/Dextrocardia" title="Dextrocardia">offset to the right</a>) and is felt to be on the left because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_heart" title="Left heart">left heart</a> is stronger and larger, since it pumps to all body parts. Because the heart is between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_lung" title="Human lung">lungs</a>, the left lung is smaller than the right lung and has a cardiac notch in its border to accommodate the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
The heart is cone-shaped, with its <b>base</b> positioned upwards and tapering down to the apex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> An adult heart has a mass of 250–350 grams (9–12 oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[16]</a></sup> The heart is often described as the size of a fist: 12 cm (5 in) in length, 8 cm (3.5 in) wide, and 6 cm (2.5 in) in thickness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> although this description is disputed, as the heart is likely to be slightly larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[17]</a></sup> Well-trained <a href="/wiki/Athlete" title="Athlete">athletes</a> can have much larger hearts due to the effects of exercise on the heart muscle, similar to the response of skeletal muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chambers">Chambers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heart_diseccion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Heart_diseccion.jpg/220px-Heart_diseccion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Heart_diseccion.jpg/330px-Heart_diseccion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Heart_diseccion.jpg/440px-Heart_diseccion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heart_diseccion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heart being dissected showing right and left ventricles, from above</div></div></div>
<p>The heart has four chambers, two upper <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">atria</a>, the receiving chambers, and two lower <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricles</a>, the discharging chambers. The atria open into the ventricles via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_valve" title="Atrioventricular valve">atrioventricular valves</a>, present in the <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_septum" title="Atrioventricular septum">atrioventricular septum</a>. This distinction is visible also on the surface of the heart as the <a href="/wiki/Coronary_sulcus" title="Coronary sulcus">coronary sulcus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960–962_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960–962-24">[18]</a></sup> There is an ear-shaped structure in the upper right atrium called the <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)#Structure" title="Atrium (heart)">right atrial appendage</a>, or auricle, and another in the upper left atrium, the <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)#Structure" title="Atrium (heart)">left atrial appendage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008964–967_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008964–967-25">[19]</a></sup> The right atrium and the right ventricle together are sometimes referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_heart" title="Right heart">right heart</a>. Similarly, the left atrium and the left ventricle together are sometimes referred to as the left heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960-12">[6]</a></sup> The ventricles are separated from each other by the <a href="/wiki/Interventricular_septum" title="Interventricular septum">interventricular septum</a>, visible on the surface of the heart as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_longitudinal_sulcus" title="Anterior longitudinal sulcus">anterior longitudinal sulcus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Posterior_interventricular_sulcus" title="Posterior interventricular sulcus">posterior interventricular sulcus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960–962_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008960–962-24">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Dense_connective_tissue" title="Dense connective tissue">fibrous</a> <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_skeleton" title="Cardiac skeleton">cardiac skeleton</a> gives structure to the heart. It forms the atrioventricular septum, which separates the atria from the ventricles, and the fibrous rings, which serve as bases for the four <a href="/wiki/Heart_valve" title="Heart valve">heart valves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[20]</a></sup> The cardiac skeleton also provides an important boundary in the heart's electrical conduction system since collagen cannot conduct <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. The interatrial septum separates the atria, and the interventricular septum separates the ventricles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The interventricular septum is much thicker than the interatrial septum since the ventricles need to generate greater pressure when they contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-9"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Valves">Valves</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_valves" title="Heart valves">Heart valves</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:422px;max-width:422px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2011_Heart_Valves.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1217" data-file-width="1525" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/2011_Heart_Valves.jpg/230px-2011_Heart_Valves.jpg" four valves are clearly visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-10"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:186px;max-width:186px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_the_human_heart_(cropped).svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="663" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/184px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/276px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/368px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png 2x" width="184"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The heart, showing valves, arteries and veins. The white arrows show the normal direction of blood flow.</div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1112" data-file-width="1606" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg/220px-2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg/330px-2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg/440px-2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2010_Chordae_Tendinae_Papillary_Muscles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frontal section showing <a href="/wiki/Papillary_muscle" title="Papillary muscle">papillary muscles</a> attached to the <a href="/wiki/Tricuspid_valve" title="Tricuspid valve">tricuspid valve</a> on the right and to the <a href="/wiki/Mitral_valve" title="Mitral valve">mitral valve</a> on the left via <a href="/wiki/Chordae_tendineae" title="Chordae tendineae">chordae tendineae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-11"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The heart has four valves, which separate its chambers. One valve lies between each atrium and ventricle, and one valve rests at the exit of each ventricle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-12"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The valves between the atria and ventricles are called the atrioventricular valves. Between the right atrium and the right ventricle is the <a href="/wiki/Tricuspid_valve" title="Tricuspid valve">tricuspid valve</a>. The tricuspid valve has three cusps,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008966–967_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008966–967-27">[21]</a></sup> which connect to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordae_tendinae" title="Chordae tendinae">chordae tendinae</a> and three <a href="/wiki/Papillary_muscle" title="Papillary muscle">papillary muscles</a> named the anterior, posterior, and septal muscles, after their relative positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008966–967_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008966–967-27">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mitral_valve" title="Mitral valve">mitral valve</a> lies between the left atrium and left ventricle. It is also known as the bicuspid valve due to its having two cusps, an anterior and a posterior cusp. These cusps are also attached via chordae tendinae to two papillary muscles projecting from the ventricular wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008970_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008970-28">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The papillary muscles extend from the walls of the heart to valves by cartilaginous connections called chordae tendinae. These muscles prevent the valves from falling too far back when they close.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[23]</a></sup> During the relaxation phase of the cardiac cycle, the papillary muscles are also relaxed and the tension on the chordae tendineae is slight. As the heart chambers contract, so do the papillary muscles. This creates tension on the chordae tendineae, helping to hold the cusps of the atrioventricular valves in place and preventing them from being blown back into the atria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-13"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[g]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008966–967_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008966–967-27">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two additional semilunar valves sit at the exit of each of the ventricles. The <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_valve" title="Pulmonary valve">pulmonary valve</a> is located at the base of the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a>. This has three cusps which are not attached to any papillary muscles. When the ventricle relaxes blood flows back into the ventricle from the artery and this flow of blood fills the pocket-like valve, pressing against the cusps which close to seal the valve. The semilunar <a href="/wiki/Aortic_valve" title="Aortic valve">aortic valve</a> is at the base of the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> and also is not attached to papillary muscles. This too has three cusps which close with the pressure of the blood flowing back from the aorta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-14"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_heart">Right heart</span></h4>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Right_heart"></span>
The right heart consists of two chambers, the right atrium and the right ventricle, separated by a valve, the <a href="/wiki/Tricuspid_valve" title="Tricuspid valve">tricuspid valve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-15"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The right atrium receives blood almost continuously from the body's two major <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Superior_vena_cava" title="Superior vena cava">superior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vena_cava" title="Vena cava">venae cavae</a>. A small amount of blood from the coronary circulation also drains into the right atrium via the <a href="/wiki/Coronary_sinus" title="Coronary sinus">coronary sinus</a>, which is immediately above and to the middle of the opening of the inferior vena cava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-16"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> In the wall of the right atrium is an oval-shaped depression known as the <a href="/wiki/Fossa_ovalis_(heart)" title="Fossa ovalis (heart)">fossa ovalis</a>, which is a remnant of an opening in the fetal heart known as the <a href="/wiki/Foramen_ovale_(heart)" title="Foramen ovale (heart)">foramen ovale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-17"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Most of the internal surface of the right atrium is smooth, the depression of the fossa ovalis is medial, and the anterior surface has prominent ridges of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pectinate_muscle" title="Pectinate muscle">pectinate muscles</a>, which are also present in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_atrial_appendage" title="Right atrial appendage">right atrial appendage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-18"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The right atrium is connected to the right ventricle by the tricuspid valve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-19"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The walls of the right ventricle are lined with <a href="/wiki/Trabeculae_carneae" title="Trabeculae carneae">trabeculae carneae</a>, ridges of cardiac muscle covered by endocardium. In addition to these muscular ridges, a band of cardiac muscle, also covered by endocardium, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderator_band" title="Moderator band">moderator band</a> reinforces the thin walls of the right ventricle and plays a crucial role in cardiac conduction. It arises from the lower part of the interventricular septum and crosses the interior space of the right ventricle to connect with the inferior papillary muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-20"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The right ventricle tapers into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_trunk" title="Pulmonary trunk">pulmonary trunk</a>, into which it ejects blood when contracting. The pulmonary trunk branches into the left and right pulmonary arteries that carry the blood to each lung. The pulmonary valve lies between the right heart and the pulmonary trunk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-21"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Left_heart">Left heart</span></h4>
<p><span class="anchor" id="left_heart"></span>
The left heart has two chambers: the left atrium and the left ventricle, separated by the <a href="/wiki/Mitral_valve" title="Mitral valve">mitral valve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-22"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The left atrium receives oxygenated blood back from the lungs via one of the four <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_vein" title="Pulmonary vein">pulmonary veins</a>. The left atrium has an outpouching called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_atrial_appendage" title="Left atrial appendage">left atrial appendage</a>. Like the right atrium, the left atrium is lined by <a href="/wiki/Pectinate_muscles" title="Pectinate muscles">pectinate muscles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[24]</a></sup> The left atrium is connected to the left ventricle by the mitral valve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-23"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The left ventricle is much thicker as compared with the right, due to the greater force needed to pump blood to the entire body. Like the right ventricle, the left also has <a href="/wiki/Trabeculae_carneae" title="Trabeculae carneae">trabeculae carneae</a>, but there is no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderator_band" title="Moderator band">moderator band</a>. The left ventricle pumps blood to the body through the aortic valve and into the aorta. Two small openings above the aortic valve carry blood to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_muscle" title="Heart muscle">heart muscle</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Left_coronary_artery" title="Left coronary artery">left coronary artery</a> is above the left cusp of the valve, and the <a href="/wiki/Right_coronary_artery" title="Right coronary artery">right coronary artery</a> is above the right cusp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-24"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wall">Wall</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle" title="Cardiac muscle">Cardiac muscle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2004_Heart_Wall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1942" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2004_Heart_Wall.jpg/220px-2004_Heart_Wall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2004_Heart_Wall.jpg/330px-2004_Heart_Wall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2004_Heart_Wall.jpg/440px-2004_Heart_Wall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2004_Heart_Wall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Layers of the heart wall, including visceral and parietal pericardium</div></div></div>
<p>The heart wall is made up of three layers: the inner <a href="/wiki/Endocardium" title="Endocardium">endocardium</a>, middle <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myocardium" title="Myocardium">myocardium</a> and outer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicardium" title="Epicardium">epicardium</a>. These are surrounded by a double-membraned sac called the pericardium.
</p><p>The innermost layer of the heart is called the endocardium. It is made up of a lining of <a href="/wiki/Simple_squamous_epithelium" title="Simple squamous epithelium">simple squamous epithelium</a> and covers heart chambers and valves. It is continuous with the <a href="/wiki/Endothelium" title="Endothelium">endothelium</a> of the veins and arteries of the heart, and is joined to the myocardium with a thin layer of connective tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-25"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The endocardium, by secreting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothelins" title="Endothelins">endothelins</a>, may also play a role in regulating the contraction of the myocardium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-26"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1014" data-file-width="1025" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg/200px-2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg/300px-2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg/400px-2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2006_Heart_Musculature.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The swirling pattern of myocardium helps the heart pump effectively</div></div></div><p>The middle layer of the heart wall is the myocardium, which is the <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle" title="Cardiac muscle">cardiac muscle</a>—a layer of involuntary <a href="/wiki/Striated_muscle_tissue" title="Striated muscle tissue">striated muscle tissue</a> surrounded by a framework of <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>. The cardiac muscle pattern is elegant and complex, as the muscle cells swirl and spiral around the chambers of the heart, with the outer muscles forming a figure 8 pattern around the atria and around the bases of the great vessels and the inner muscles, forming a figure 8 around the two ventricles and proceeding toward the apex. This complex swirling pattern allows the heart to pump blood more effectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-27"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two types of cells in cardiac muscle: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiomyocyte" title="Cardiomyocyte">muscle cells</a> which have the ability to contract easily, and <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_pacemaker" title="Cardiac pacemaker">pacemaker cells</a> of the conducting system. The muscle cells make up the bulk (99%) of cells in the atria and ventricles. These contractile cells are connected by <a href="/wiki/Intercalated_disc" title="Intercalated disc">intercalated discs</a> which allow a rapid response to impulses of <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_action_potential" title="Cardiac action potential">action potential</a> from the pacemaker cells. The intercalated discs allow the cells to act as a <a href="/wiki/Syncytium" title="Syncytium">syncytium</a> and enable the contractions that pump blood through the heart and into the <a href="/wiki/Great_arteries" title="Great arteries">major arteries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-28"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The pacemaker cells make up 1% of cells and form the conduction system of the heart. They are generally much smaller than the contractile cells and have few <a href="/wiki/Myofibril" title="Myofibril">myofibrils</a> which gives them limited contractibility. Their function is similar in many respects to <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-29"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Cardiac muscle tissue has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autorhythmicity" title="Autorhythmicity">autorhythmicity</a>, the unique ability to initiate a cardiac action potential at a fixed rate—spreading the impulse rapidly from cell to cell to trigger the contraction of the entire heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-30"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are specific <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics#Gene_and_protein_expression" title="Bioinformatics">proteins expressed</a> in cardiac muscle cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[26]</a></sup> These are mostly associated with muscle contraction, and bind with <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tropomyosin" title="Tropomyosin">tropomyosin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Troponin" title="Troponin">troponin</a>. They include <a href="/wiki/MYH6" title="MYH6">MYH6</a>, <a href="/wiki/ACTC1" title="ACTC1">ACTC1</a>, <a href="/wiki/TNNI3" title="TNNI3">TNNI3</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CDH2" title="CDH2">CDH2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plakophilin-2" title="Plakophilin-2">PKP2</a>. Other proteins expressed are <a href="/wiki/MYH7" title="MYH7">MYH7</a> and <a href="/wiki/LDB3" title="LDB3">LDB3</a> that are also expressed in skeletal muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pericardium">Pericardium</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">Pericardium</a></div>
<p>The pericardium is the sac that surrounds the heart. The tough outer surface of the pericardium is called the fibrous membrane. This is lined by a double inner membrane called the serous membrane that produces <a href="/wiki/Pericardial_fluid" title="Pericardial fluid">pericardial fluid</a> to lubricate the surface of the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008959_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008959-35">[28]</a></sup> The part of the serous membrane attached to the fibrous membrane is called the parietal pericardium, while the part of the serous membrane attached to the heart is known as the visceral pericardium. The pericardium is present in order to lubricate its movement against other structures within the chest, to keep the heart's position stabilised within the chest, and to protect the heart from infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coronary_circulation">Coronary circulation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coronary_arteries.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="1047" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Coronary_arteries.svg/220px-Coronary_arteries.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Coronary_arteries.svg/330px-Coronary_arteries.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Coronary_arteries.svg/440px-Coronary_arteries.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coronary_arteries.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arterial supply to the heart (red), with other areas labelled (blue).</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coronary_circulation" title="Coronary circulation">Coronary circulation</a></div>
<p>Heart tissue, like all cells in the body, needs to be supplied with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> and a way of removing <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste" title="Metabolic waste">metabolic wastes</a>. This is achieved by the coronary circulation, which includes <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymph_vessel" title="Lymph vessel">lymphatic vessels</a>. Blood flow through the coronary vessels occurs in peaks and troughs relating to the heart muscle's relaxation or contraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-31"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Heart tissue receives blood from two arteries which arise just above the aortic valve. These are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_main_coronary_artery" title="Left main coronary artery">left main coronary artery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Right_coronary_artery" title="Right coronary artery">right coronary artery</a>. The left main coronary artery splits shortly after leaving the aorta into two vessels, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_anterior_descending" title="Left anterior descending">left anterior descending</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_circumflex_artery" title="Left circumflex artery">left circumflex artery</a>. The left anterior descending artery supplies heart tissue and the front, outer side, and septum of the left ventricle. It does this by branching into smaller arteries—diagonal and septal branches. The left circumflex supplies the back and underneath of the left ventricle. The right coronary artery supplies the right atrium, right ventricle, and lower posterior sections of the left ventricle. The right coronary artery also supplies blood to the atrioventricular node (in about 90% of people) and the sinoatrial node (in about 60% of people). The right coronary artery runs in a groove at the back of the heart and the left anterior descending artery runs in a groove at the front. There is significant variation between people in the anatomy of the arteries that supply the heart <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010525_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010525-37">[30]</a></sup> The arteries divide at their furthest reaches into smaller branches that join at the edges of each arterial distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-32"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Coronary_sinus" title="Coronary sinus">coronary sinus</a> is a large vein that drains into the right atrium, and receives most of the venous drainage of the heart. It receives blood from the <a href="/wiki/Great_cardiac_vein" title="Great cardiac vein">great cardiac vein</a> (receiving the left atrium and both ventricles), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posterior_cardiac_vein" title="Posterior cardiac vein">posterior cardiac vein</a> (draining the back of the left ventricle), the <a href="/wiki/Middle_cardiac_vein" title="Middle cardiac vein">middle cardiac vein</a> (draining the bottom of the left and right ventricles), and <a href="/wiki/Small_cardiac_vein" title="Small cardiac vein">small cardiac veins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008981_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008981-38">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anterior_cardiac_veins" title="Anterior cardiac veins">anterior cardiac veins</a> drain the front of the right ventricle and drain directly into the right atrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-33"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Small lymphatic networks called <a href="/wiki/Plexus" title="Plexus">plexuses</a> exist beneath each of the three layers of the heart. These networks collect into a main left and a main right trunk, which travel up the groove between the ventricles that exists on the heart's surface, receiving smaller vessels as they travel up. These vessels then travel into the atrioventricular groove, and receive a third vessel which drains the section of the left ventricle sitting on the diaphragm. The left vessel joins with this third vessel, and travels along the pulmonary artery and left atrium, ending in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_tracheobronchial_node" title="Inferior tracheobronchial node">inferior tracheobronchial node</a>. The right vessel travels along the right atrium and the part of the right ventricle sitting on the diaphragm. It usually then travels in front of the ascending aorta and then ends in a brachiocephalic node.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008982_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008982-39">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nerve_supply">Nerve supply</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2071" data-file-width="1110" decoding="async" height="410" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg/220px-2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg/330px-2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg/440px-2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2032_Automatic_Innervation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Autonomic innervation of the heart</div></div></div>
<p>The heart receives nerve signals from the <a href="/wiki/Vagus_nerve" title="Vagus nerve">vagus nerve</a> and from nerves arising from the <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_trunk" title="Sympathetic trunk">sympathetic trunk</a>. These nerves act to influence, but not control, the heart rate. <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">Sympathetic nerves</a> also influence the force of heart contraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010526_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010526-40">[33]</a></sup> Signals that travel along these nerves arise from two paired <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_centre" title="Cardiovascular centre">cardiovascular centres</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Medulla_oblongata" title="Medulla oblongata">medulla oblongata</a>. The vagus nerve of the <a href="/wiki/Parasympathetic_nervous_system" title="Parasympathetic nervous system">parasympathetic nervous system</a> acts to decrease the heart rate, and nerves from the <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_trunk" title="Sympathetic trunk">sympathetic trunk</a> act to increase the heart rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-34"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> These nerves form a network of nerves that lies over the heart called the <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_plexus" title="Cardiac plexus">cardiac plexus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-35"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008982_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008982-39">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The vagus nerve is a long, wandering nerve that emerges from the <a href="/wiki/Brainstem" title="Brainstem">brainstem</a> and provides parasympathetic stimulation to a large number of organs in the thorax and abdomen, including the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008945_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy2008945-41">[34]</a></sup> The nerves from the sympathetic trunk emerge through the T1-T4 <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_ganglia" title="Thoracic ganglia">thoracic ganglia</a> and travel to both the sinoatrial and atrioventricular nodes, as well as to the atria and ventricles. The ventricles are more richly innervated by sympathetic fibers than parasympathetic fibers. Sympathetic stimulation causes the release of the neurotransmitter <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">norepinephrine</a> (also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noradrenaline" title="Noradrenaline">noradrenaline</a>) at the <a href="/wiki/Neuromuscular_junction" title="Neuromuscular junction">neuromuscular junction</a> of the cardiac nerves. This shortens the repolarization period, thus speeding the rate of depolarization and contraction, which results in an increased heart rate. It opens chemical or ligand-gated sodium and calcium ion channels, allowing an influx of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">positively charged ions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-36"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Norepinephrine binds to the <a href="/wiki/Beta-1_adrenergic_receptor" title="Beta-1 adrenergic receptor">beta–1 receptor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-37"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Early_development"></span><span class="anchor" id="Heart_development"></span><span class="anchor" id="Development"></span><span class="anchor" id="Embryology"></span>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Heart_development" title="Heart development">Heart development</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryogenesis" title="Human embryogenesis">Human embryogenesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1954" data-file-width="2267" decoding="async" height="388" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg/450px-2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg/675px-2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg/900px-2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2037_Embryonic_Development_of_Heart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Development of the human heart during the first eight weeks (top) and the formation of the heart chambers (bottom). In this figure, the blue and red colors represent blood inflow and outflow (not venous and arterial blood). Initially, all venous blood flows from the tail/atria to the ventricles/head, a very different pattern from that of an adult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-38"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The heart is the first functional organ to develop and starts to beat and pump blood at about three weeks into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryogenesis" title="Human embryogenesis">embryogenesis</a>. This early start is crucial for subsequent embryonic and <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">prenatal development</a>.
</p><p>The heart derives from <a href="/wiki/Splanchnopleuric_mesenchyme" title="Splanchnopleuric mesenchyme">splanchnopleuric mesenchyme</a> in the neural plate which forms the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiogenic_region" title="Cardiogenic region">cardiogenic region</a>. Two <a href="/wiki/Endocardial_tubes" title="Endocardial tubes">endocardial tubes</a> form here that fuse to form a primitive heart tube known as the <a href="/wiki/Tubular_heart" title="Tubular heart">tubular heart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[35]</a></sup> Between the third and fourth week, the heart tube lengthens, and begins to fold to form an S-shape within the pericardium. This places the chambers and major vessels into the correct alignment for the developed heart. Further development will include the formation of the septa and the valves and the remodeling of the heart chambers. By the end of the fifth week, the septa are complete, and by the ninth week, the heart valves are complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-39"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the fifth week, there is an opening in the fetal heart known as the <a href="/wiki/Foramen_ovale_(heart)" title="Foramen ovale (heart)">foramen ovale</a>. The foramen ovale allowed blood in the fetal heart to pass directly from the right atrium to the left atrium, allowing some blood to bypass the lungs. Within seconds after birth, a flap of tissue known as the <a href="/wiki/Septum_primum" title="Septum primum">septum primum</a> that previously acted as a valve closes the foramen ovale and establishes the typical cardiac circulation pattern. A depression in the surface of the right atrium remains where the foramen ovale was, called the fossa ovalis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-40"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryonic</a> heart begins beating at around 22 days after conception (5 weeks after the last normal menstrual period, LMP). It starts to beat at a rate near to the mother's which is about 75–80 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beats_per_minute" title="Beats per minute">beats per minute</a> (bpm). The embryonic heart rate then accelerates and reaches a peak rate of 165–185 bpm early in the early 7th week (early 9th week after the LMP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fetal_Sonography_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fetal_Sonography-44">[37]</a></sup> After 9 weeks (start of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetal" title="Fetal">fetal</a> stage) it starts to decelerate, slowing to around 145 (±25) bpm at birth. There is no difference in female and male heart rates before birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[38]</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Function"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_physiology" title="Cardiac physiology">Cardiac physiology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_flow">Blood flow</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latidos.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Latidos.gif/220px-Latidos.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/21/Latidos.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latidos.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blood flow through the valves</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="471" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Flow_through_the_Heart.webm" height="124" id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Flow_through_the_Heart.webm/220px--Flow_through_the_Heart.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="186272" data-framerate="30" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e5/Flow_through_the_Heart.webm/Flow_through_the_Heart.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
