<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="91" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Schematic_3D_illustration_of_blood_flow_through_the_heart.ogv" height="124" id="mwe_player_4" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Schematic_3D_illustration_of_blood_flow_through_the_heart.ogv/220px--Schematic_3D_illustration_of_blood_flow_through_the_heart.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="2575755" data-framerate="60" data-height="1080" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file, 1,920 × 1,080 (2.58 Mbps)" data-width="1920" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Schematic_3D_illustration_of_blood_flow_through_the_heart.ogv" type='video/ogg; codecs="theora, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schematic_3D_illustration_of_blood_flow_through_the_heart.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Video explanation of blood flow through the heart</div></div></div>
<p>The heart functions as a pump in the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> to provide a continuous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_flow" title="Blood flow">flow of blood</a> throughout the body. This circulation consists of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systemic_circulation" title="Systemic circulation">systemic circulation</a> to and from the body and the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a> to and from the lungs. Blood in the pulmonary circulation exchanges <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> for oxygen in the lungs through the process of <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">respiration</a>. The systemic circulation then transports oxygen to the body and returns carbon dioxide and relatively deoxygenated blood to the heart for transfer to the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-41"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Heart#Right_heart" title="Heart">right heart</a> collects deoxygenated blood from two large veins, the <a href="/wiki/Superior_vena_cava" title="Superior vena cava">superior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior</a> <a href="/wiki/Venae_cavae" title="Venae cavae">venae cavae</a>. Blood collects in the right and left atrium continuously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-42"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The superior vena cava drains blood from above the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a> and empties into the upper back part of the right atrium. The inferior vena cava drains the blood from below the diaphragm and empties into the back part of the atrium below the opening for the superior vena cava. Immediately above and to the middle of the opening of the inferior vena cava is the opening of the thin-walled coronary sinus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-43"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/Coronary_sinus" title="Coronary sinus">coronary sinus</a> returns deoxygenated blood from the myocardium to the right atrium. The blood collects in the right atrium. When the right atrium contracts, the blood is pumped through the <a href="/wiki/Tricuspid_valve" title="Tricuspid valve">tricuspid valve</a> into the right ventricle. As the right ventricle contracts, the tricuspid valve closes and the blood is pumped into the pulmonary trunk through the pulmonary valve. The pulmonary trunk divides into pulmonary arteries and progressively smaller arteries throughout the lungs, until it reaches <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capillaries" title="Capillaries">capillaries</a>. As these pass by <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a> carbon dioxide is <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">exchanged</a> for oxygen. This happens through the passive process of <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a>.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Heart#Left_heart" title="Heart">left heart</a>, oxygenated blood is returned to the left atrium via the pulmonary veins. It is then pumped into the left ventricle through the <a href="/wiki/Mitral_valve" title="Mitral valve">mitral valve</a> and into the aorta through the aortic valve for systemic circulation. The aorta is a large artery that branches into many smaller arteries, <a href="/wiki/Arteriole" title="Arteriole">arterioles</a>, and ultimately capillaries. In the capillaries, oxygen and nutrients from blood are supplied to body cells for metabolism, and exchanged for carbon dioxide and waste products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-44"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Capillary blood, now deoxygenated, travels into <a href="/wiki/Venule" title="Venule">venules</a> and veins that ultimately collect in the superior and inferior vena cavae, and into the right heart.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiac_cycle">Cardiac cycle</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_cycle" title="Cardiac cycle">Cardiac cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Systole" title="Systole">Systole</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diastole" title="Diastole">Diastole</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2192" data-file-width="2013" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg/220px-2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg/330px-2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg/440px-2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2027_Phases_of_the_Cardiac_Cycle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cardiac cycle as correlated to the ECG</div></div></div>
<p>The cardiac cycle is the sequence of events in which the heart contracts and relaxes with every heartbeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011105–107_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011105–107-15">[9]</a></sup> The period of time during which the ventricles contract, forcing blood out into the aorta and main pulmonary artery, is known as <a href="/wiki/Systole" title="Systole">systole</a>, while the period during which the ventricles relax and refill with blood is known as <a href="/wiki/Diastole" title="Diastole">diastole</a>. The atria and ventricles work in concert, so in systole when the ventricles are contracting, the atria are relaxed and collecting blood. When the ventricles are relaxed in diastole, the atria contract to pump blood to the ventricles. This coordination ensures blood is pumped efficiently to the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-45"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the beginning of the cardiac cycle, the ventricles are relaxing. As they do so, they are filled by blood passing through the open <a href="/wiki/Mitral_valve" title="Mitral valve">mitral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tricuspid_valve" title="Tricuspid valve">tricuspid</a> valves. After the ventricles have completed most of their filling, the atria contract, forcing further blood into the ventricles and priming the pump. Next, the ventricles start to contract. As the pressure rises within the cavities of the ventricles, the mitral and tricuspid valves are forced shut. As the pressure within the ventricles rises further, exceeding the pressure with the aorta and pulmonary arteries, the aortic and pulmonary valves open. Blood is ejected from the heart, causing the pressure within the ventricles to fall. Simultaneously, the atria refill as blood flows into the right atrium through the superior and <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior vena cavae</a>, and into the left atrium through the pulmonary veins. Finally, when the pressure within the ventricles falls below the pressure within the aorta and pulmonary arteries, the aortic and pulmonary valves close. The ventricles start to relax, the mitral and tricuspid valves open, and the cycle begins again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011105–107_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011105–107-15">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiac_output">Cardiac output</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_output" title="Cardiac output">Cardiac output</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1219" data-file-width="1612" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg/220px-2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg/330px-2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg/440px-2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2029_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Heart_Sounds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The x-axis reflects time with a recording of the heart sounds. The y-axis represents pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-46"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Cardiac output (CO) is a measurement of the amount of blood pumped by each ventricle (stroke volume) in one minute. This is calculated by multiplying the stroke volume (SV) by the beats per minute of the heart rate (HR). So that: CO = SV x HR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-47"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
The cardiac output is normalized to body size through <a href="/wiki/Body_surface_area" title="Body surface area">body surface area</a> and is called the <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_index" title="Cardiac index">cardiac index</a>.
</p><p>The average cardiac output, using an average stroke volume of about 70mL, is 5.25 L/min, with a normal range of 4.0–8.0 L/min.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-48"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The stroke volume is normally measured using an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echocardiogram" title="Echocardiogram">echocardiogram</a> and can be influenced by the size of the heart, physical and mental condition of the individual, <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_contractility" title="Myocardial contractility">contractility</a>, duration of contraction, <a href="/wiki/Preload_(cardiology)" title="Preload (cardiology)">preload</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afterload" title="Afterload">afterload</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-49"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Preload_(cardiology)" title="Preload (cardiology)">Preload</a> refers to the filling pressure of the atria at the end of diastole, when the ventricles are at their fullest. A main factor is how long it takes the ventricles to fill: if the ventricles contract more frequently, then there is less time to fill and the preload will be less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-50"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Preload can also be affected by a person's blood volume. The force of each contraction of the heart muscle is proportional to the preload, described as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank-Starling_mechanism" title="Frank-Starling mechanism">Frank-Starling mechanism</a>. This states that the force of contraction is directly proportional to the initial length of muscle fiber, meaning a ventricle will contract more forcefully, the more it is stretched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-51"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011110–113_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011110–113-46">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Afterload" title="Afterload">Afterload</a>, or how much pressure the heart must generate to eject blood at systole, is influenced by <a href="/wiki/Vascular_resistance" title="Vascular resistance">vascular resistance</a>. It can be influenced by narrowing of the heart valves (<a href="/wiki/Stenosis" title="Stenosis">stenosis</a>) or contraction or relaxation of the peripheral blood vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-52"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The strength of heart muscle contractions controls the stroke volume. This can be influenced positively or negatively by agents termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inotropes" title="Inotropes">inotropes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERRY2010_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-BERRY2010-47">[40]</a></sup> These agents can be a result of changes within the body, or be given as drugs as part of treatment for a medical disorder, or as a form of <a href="/wiki/Life_support" title="Life support">life support</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Intensive_care_unit" title="Intensive care unit">intensive care units</a>. Inotropes that increase the force of contraction are "positive" inotropes, and include <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">sympathetic</a> agents such as <a href="/wiki/Adrenaline" title="Adrenaline">adrenaline</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noradrenaline" title="Noradrenaline">noradrenaline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OH2010_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-OH2010-48">[41]</a></sup> "Negative" inotropes decrease the force of contraction and include <a href="/wiki/Calcium_channel_blocker" title="Calcium channel blocker">calcium channel blockers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERRY2010_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-BERRY2010-47">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_conduction">Electrical conduction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conduction_system_of_the_heart" title="Electrical conduction system of the heart">Electrical conduction system of the heart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heart_rate" title="Heart rate">Heart rate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ECG_principle_slow.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/ECG_principle_slow.gif/275px-ECG_principle_slow.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/ECG_principle_slow.gif/413px-ECG_principle_slow.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/ECG_principle_slow.gif/550px-ECG_principle_slow.gif 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ECG_principle_slow.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transmission of a <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_action_potential" title="Cardiac action potential">cardiac action potential</a> through the heart's conduction system</div></div></div>
<p>The normal rhythmical heart beat, called <a href="/wiki/Sinus_rhythm" title="Sinus rhythm">sinus rhythm</a>, is established by the heart's own pacemaker, the <a href="/wiki/Sinoatrial_node" title="Sinoatrial node">sinoatrial node</a> (also known as the sinus node or the SA node). Here an electrical signal is created that travels through the heart, causing the heart muscle to contract. The sinoatrial node is found in the upper part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_atrium" title="Right atrium">right atrium</a> near to the junction with the superior vena cava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[42]</a></sup> The electrical signal generated by the sinoatrial node travels through the right atrium in a radial way that is not completely understood. It travels to the left atrium via <a href="/wiki/Bachmann%27s_bundle" title="Bachmann's bundle">Bachmann's bundle</a>, such that the muscles of the left and right atria contract together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup> The signal then travels to the <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_node" title="Atrioventricular node">atrioventricular node</a>. This is found at the bottom of the right atrium in the <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_septum" title="Atrioventricular septum">atrioventricular septum</a>, the boundary between the right atrium and the left ventricle. The septum is part of the <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_skeleton" title="Cardiac skeleton">cardiac skeleton</a>, tissue within the heart that the electrical signal cannot pass through, which forces the signal to pass through the atrioventricular node only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-53"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> The signal then travels along the <a href="/wiki/Bundle_of_His" title="Bundle of His">bundle of His</a> to left and right <a href="/wiki/Bundle_branches" title="Bundle branches">bundle branches</a> through to the ventricles of the heart. In the ventricles the signal is carried by specialized tissue called the <a href="/wiki/Purkinje_fibers" title="Purkinje fibers">Purkinje fibers</a> which then transmit the electric charge to the heart muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1417" data-file-width="2446" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg/275px-ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg/413px-ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg/550px-ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg.png 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ConductionsystemoftheheartwithouttheHeart-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Conduction system of the heart</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_rate">Heart rate</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heart_rate" title="Heart rate">Heart rate</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">

<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Emily%27s_racing_heartbeat.wav" title="File:Emily's racing heartbeat.wav">A racing heartbeat</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="88" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Emily's_racing_heartbeat.wav" id="mwe_player_5" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="68944" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/09/Emily%27s_racing_heartbeat.wav/Emily%27s_racing_heartbeat.wav.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="111216" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/09/Emily%27s_racing_heartbeat.wav/Emily%27s_racing_heartbeat.wav.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><source data-bandwidth="1411200" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="WAV source" data-title="Original WAV file (1.41 Mbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Emily%27s_racing_heartbeat.wav" type="audio/wav"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">Heart sounds of a 16 year old girl immediately after running, with a heart rate of 186 BPM.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1667" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg/220px-2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg/330px-2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg/440px-2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2020_SA_Node_Tracing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The prepotential is due to a slow influx of sodium ions until the threshold is reached followed by a rapid depolarization and repolarization. The prepotential accounts for the membrane reaching threshold and initiates the spontaneous depolarization and contraction of the cell; there is no resting potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-54"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The normal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resting_heart_rate" title="Resting heart rate">resting heart rate</a> is called the <a href="/wiki/Sinus_rhythm" title="Sinus rhythm">sinus rhythm</a>, created and sustained by the <a href="/wiki/Sinoatrial_node" title="Sinoatrial node">sinoatrial node</a>, a group of pacemaking cells found in the wall of the right atrium. Cells in the sinoatrial node do this by creating an <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potential</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_action_potential" title="Cardiac action potential">cardiac action potential</a> is created by the movement of specific <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a> into and out of the pacemaker cells. The action potential then spreads to nearby cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011115–120_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011115–120-54">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the sinoatrial cells are resting, they have a negative charge on their membranes. A rapid influx of <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> ions causes the membrane's charge to become positive; this is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Depolarisation" title="Depolarisation">depolarisation</a> and occurs spontaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-55"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Once the cell has a sufficiently high charge, the sodium channels close and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> ions then begin to enter the cell, shortly after which <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> begins to leave it. All the ions travel through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_channels" title="Ion channels">ion channels</a> in the membrane of the sinoatrial cells. The potassium and calcium start to move out of and into the cell only once it has a sufficiently high charge, and so are called <a href="/wiki/Voltage-gated_calcium_channel" title="Voltage-gated calcium channel">voltage-gated</a>. Shortly after this, the calcium channels close and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potassium_channels" title="Potassium channels">potassium channels</a> open, allowing potassium to leave the cell. This causes the cell to have a negative resting charge and is called <a href="/wiki/Repolarization" title="Repolarization">repolarization</a>. When the membrane potential reaches approximately −60 mV, the potassium channels close and the process may begin again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-56"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ions move from areas where they are concentrated to where they are not. For this reason sodium moves into the cell from outside, and potassium moves from within the cell to outside the cell. Calcium also plays a critical role. Their influx through slow channels means that the sinoatrial cells have a prolonged "plateau" phase when they have a positive charge. A part of this is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_refractory_period" title="Absolute refractory period">absolute refractory period</a>. Calcium ions also combine with the regulatory protein <a href="/wiki/Troponin_C" title="Troponin C">troponin C</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troponin_complex" title="Troponin complex">troponin complex</a> to enable <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">contraction</a> of the cardiac muscle, and separate from the protein to allow relaxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-55">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The adult resting heart rate ranges from 60 to 100 bpm. The resting heart rate of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neonate" title="Neonate">newborn</a> can be 129 beats per minute (bpm) and this gradually decreases until maturity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[49]</a></sup> An athlete's heart rate can be lower than 60 bpm. During exercise the rate can be 150 bpm with maximum rates reaching from 200 to 220 bpm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-57"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h4>
<p>The normal <a href="/wiki/Sinus_rhythm" title="Sinus rhythm">sinus rhythm</a> of the heart, giving the resting heart rate, is influenced by a number of factors. The <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_centre" title="Cardiovascular centre">cardiovascular centres</a> in the brainstem control the sympathetic and parasympathetic influences to the heart through the vagus nerve and sympathetic trunk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GUYTONHALL2005_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-GUYTONHALL2005-57">[50]</a></sup> These cardiovascular centres receive input from a series of receptors including <a href="/wiki/Baroreceptor" title="Baroreceptor">baroreceptors</a>, sensing the stretching of blood vessels and <a href="/wiki/Chemoreceptor" title="Chemoreceptor">chemoreceptors</a>, sensing the amount of oxygen and carbon dioxide in the blood and its pH. Through a series of reflexes these help regulate and sustain blood flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-58"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Baroreceptors are stretch receptors located in the <a href="/wiki/Aortic_sinus" title="Aortic sinus">aortic sinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carotid_body" title="Carotid body">carotid bodies</a>, the venae cavae, and other locations, including pulmonary vessels and the right side of the heart itself. Baroreceptors fire at a rate determined by how much they are stretched,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011208_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011208-58">[51]</a></sup> which is influenced by blood pressure, level of physical activity, and the relative distribution of blood. With increased pressure and stretch, the rate of baroreceptor firing increases, and the cardiac centers decrease sympathetic stimulation and increase parasympathetic stimulation. As pressure and stretch decrease, the rate of baroreceptor firing decreases, and the cardiac centers increase sympathetic stimulation and decrease parasympathetic stimulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-59"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> There is a similar reflex, called the atrial reflex or <a href="/wiki/Bainbridge_reflex" title="Bainbridge reflex">Bainbridge reflex</a>, associated with varying rates of blood flow to the atria. Increased venous return stretches the walls of the atria where specialized baroreceptors are located. However, as the atrial baroreceptors increase their rate of firing and as they stretch due to the increased blood pressure, the cardiac center responds by increasing sympathetic stimulation and inhibiting parasympathetic stimulation to increase heart rate. The opposite is also true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-60"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Chemoreceptors present in the carotid body or adjacent to the aorta in an aortic body respond to the blood's oxygen, carbon dioxide levels. Low oxygen or high carbon dioxide will stimulate firing of the receptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011212_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuyton_&amp;_Hall2011212-59">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exercise and fitness levels, age, body temperature, <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">basal metabolic rate</a>, and even a person's emotional state can all affect the heart rate. High levels of the hormones <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a>, norepinephrine, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_hormone" title="Thyroid hormone">thyroid hormones</a> can increase the heart rate. The levels of electrolytes including calcium, potassium, and sodium can also influence the speed and regularity of the heart rate; <a href="/wiki/Hypoxemia" title="Hypoxemia">low blood oxygen</a>, low <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> may increase it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-61"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doctors_stethoscope_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1761" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Doctors_stethoscope_1.jpg/230px-Doctors_stethoscope_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Doctors_stethoscope_1.jpg/345px-Doctors_stethoscope_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Doctors_stethoscope_1.jpg/460px-Doctors_stethoscope_1.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The stethoscope is used for <a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">auscultation</a> of the heart, and is one of the most iconic symbols for <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>. A number of diseases can be detected primarily by listening for <a href="/wiki/Heart_murmur" title="Heart murmur">heart murmurs</a>.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2113ab_Atherosclerosis.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1065" data-file-width="1965" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2113ab_Atherosclerosis.jpg/230px-2113ab_Atherosclerosis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2113ab_Atherosclerosis.jpg/345px-2113ab_Atherosclerosis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2113ab_Atherosclerosis.jpg/460px-2113ab_Atherosclerosis.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">Atherosclerosis</a> is a condition affecting the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>. If the <a href="/wiki/Coronary_arteries" title="Coronary arteries">coronary arteries</a> are affected, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" title="Angina pectoris">angina pectoris</a> may result or at worse a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_attack" title="Heart attack">heart attack</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">Cardiovascular diseases</a>, which include diseases of the heart, are the leading cause of death worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOfacts_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOfacts-60">[53]</a></sup> The majority of cardiovascular disease is noncommunicable and related to lifestyle and other factors, becoming more prevalent with ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOfacts_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOfacts-60">[53]</a></sup> Heart disease is a major cause of death, accounting for an average of 30% of all deaths in 2008, globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_CVD_2013_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_CVD_2013-17">[11]</a></sup> This rate varies from a lower 28% to a high 40% in <a href="/wiki/World_Bank_high-income_economy" title="World Bank high-income economy">high-income countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrisons_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Harrisons-18">[12]</a></sup> Doctors that specialise in the heart are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiologist" title="Cardiologist">cardiologists</a>. Many other medical professionals are involved in treating diseases of the heart, including <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">doctors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiothoracic_surgeon" title="Cardiothoracic surgeon">cardiothoracic surgeons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intensivist" title="Intensivist">intensivists</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allied_health_profession" title="Allied health profession">allied health practitioners</a> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiotherapist" title="Physiotherapist">physiotherapists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietician" title="Dietician">dieticians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ischaemic_heart_disease">Ischaemic heart disease</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">Coronary artery disease</a></div>
<p>Coronary artery disease, also known as ischaemic heart disease, is caused by <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a>—a build-up of fatty material along the inner walls of the arteries. These fatty deposits known as atherosclerotic plaques <a href="/wiki/Stenosis" title="Stenosis">narrow</a> the coronary arteries, and if severe may reduce blood flow to the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHFdiseases_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHFdiseases-62">[55]</a></sup> If a narrowing (or stenosis) is relatively minor then the patient may not experience any symptoms. Severe narrowings may cause chest pain (<a href="/wiki/Angina" title="Angina">angina</a>) or breathlessness during exercise or even at rest. The thin covering of an atherosclerotic plaque can rupture, exposing the fatty centre to the circulating blood. In this case a clot or thrombus can form, blocking the artery, and restricting blood flow to an area of heart muscle causing a myocardial infarction (a heart attack) or <a href="/wiki/Unstable_angina" title="Unstable angina">unstable angina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison's20111501_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison's20111501-63">[56]</a></sup> In the worst case this may cause cardiac arrest, a sudden and utter loss of output from the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010554_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010554-64">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">Obesity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_blood_pressure" title="High blood pressure">high blood pressure</a>, uncontrolled <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, smoking and high <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> can all increase the risk of developing atherosclerosis and coronary artery disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOfacts_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHOfacts-60">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHFdiseases_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHFdiseases-62">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_failure">Heart failure</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">Heart failure</a></div>
<p>Heart failure is defined as a condition in which the heart is unable to pump enough blood to meet the demands of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-65">[58]</a></sup> Patients with <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> may experience breathlessness especially when lying flat, as well as ankle swelling, known as <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_edema" title="Peripheral edema">peripheral oedema</a>. Heart failure is the result of many diseases affecting the heart, but is most commonly associated with <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">ischaemic heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valvular_heart_disease" title="Valvular heart disease">valvular heart disease</a>, or high blood pressure. Less common causes include various <a href="/wiki/Cardiomyopathy" title="Cardiomyopathy">cardiomyopathies</a>. Heart failure is frequently associated with weakness of the heart muscle in the ventricles (systolic heart failure), but can also be seen in patients with heart muscle that is strong but stiff (diastolic heart failure). The condition may affect the left ventricle (causing predominantly breathlessness), the right ventricle (causing predominantly swelling of the legs and an elevated <a href="/wiki/Jugular_venous_pressure" title="Jugular venous pressure">jugular venous pressure</a>), or both ventricles. Patients with heart failure are at higher risk of developing dangerous heart rhythm disturbances or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_arrhythmia" title="Heart arrhythmia">arrhythmias</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-65">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiomyopathies">Cardiomyopathies</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cardiomyopathy" title="Cardiomyopathy">Cardiomyopathy</a></div><p>Cardiomyopathies are diseases affecting the muscle of the heart. Some cause abnormal thickening of the heart muscle (<a href="/wiki/Hypertrophic_cardiomyopathy" title="Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy">hypertrophic cardiomyopathy</a>), some cause the heart to abnormally expand and weaken (<a href="/wiki/Dilated_cardiomyopathy" title="Dilated cardiomyopathy">dilated cardiomyopathy</a>), some cause the heart muscle to become stiff and unable to fully relax between contractions (<a href="/wiki/Restrictive_cardiomyopathy" title="Restrictive cardiomyopathy">restrictive cardiomyopathy</a>) and some make the heart prone to abnormal heart rhythms (<a href="/wiki/Arrhythmogenic_cardiomyopathy" title="Arrhythmogenic cardiomyopathy">arrhythmogenic cardiomyopathy</a>). These conditions are often genetic and can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inherited_disease" title="Inherited disease">inherited</a>, but some such as dilated cardiomyopathy may be caused by damage from toxins such as alcohol. Some cardiomyopathies such as hypertrophic cardiomopathy are linked to a higher risk of sudden cardiac death, particularly in athletes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-62"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Many cardiomyopathies can lead to heart failure in the later stages of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-65">[58]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Valvular_heart_disease">Valvular heart disease</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Valvular_heart_disease" title="Valvular heart disease">Valvular heart disease</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Mitral_Valve_Prolapse.wav" title="File:Mitral Valve Prolapse.wav">Mitral Valve Prolapse murmur</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="12" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Mitral_Valve_Prolapse.wav" id="mwe_player_6" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="89440" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d7/Mitral_Valve_Prolapse.wav/Mitral_Valve_Prolapse.wav.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="186536" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d7/Mitral_Valve_Prolapse.wav/Mitral_Valve_Prolapse.wav.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><source data-bandwidth="1536000" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="WAV source" data-title="Original WAV file (1.54 Mbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Mitral_Valve_Prolapse.wav" type="audio/wav"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">Heart sounds of a 16 year old girl diagnosed with mitral valve prolapse and mitral regurgitation. Auscultating her heart, a systolic murmur and click is heard. Recorded with the stethoscope over the mitral valve.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div><p>Healthy heart valves allow blood to flow easily in one direction, but prevent it from flowing in the other direction. Diseased heart valves may have a narrow opening and therefore restrict the flow of blood in the forward direction (referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Aortic_stenosis" title="Aortic stenosis">stenotic valve</a>), or may allow blood to leak in the reverse direction (referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valvular_regurgitation" title="Valvular regurgitation">valvular regurgitation</a>). Valvular heart disease may cause breathlessness, blackouts, or chest pain, but may be asymptomatic and only detected on a routine examination by hearing abnormal heart sounds or a <a href="/wiki/Heart_murmur" title="Heart murmur">heart murmur</a>. In the developed world, valvular heart disease is most commonly caused by degeneration secondary to old age, but may also be caused by infection of the heart valves (<a href="/wiki/Endocarditis" title="Endocarditis">endocarditis</a>). In some parts of the world <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rheumatic_Heart_Disease" title="Rheumatic Heart Disease">rheumatic heart disease</a> is a major cause of valvular heart disease, typically leading to mitral or aortic stenosis and caused by the body's immune system reacting to a <a href="/wiki/Group_A_streptococcal_infection" title="Group A streptococcal infection">streptococcal</a> throat infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-66">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010612–13_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010612–13-67">[60]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiac_arrhythmias">Cardiac arrhythmias</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arrhythmia" title="Arrhythmia">Arrhythmia</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Extremely_irregular_heart_sounds.wav" title="File:Extremely irregular heart sounds.wav">An irregular heartbeat</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="116" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Extremely_irregular_heart_sounds.wav" id="mwe_player_7" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="88648" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/7d/Extremely_irregular_heart_sounds.wav/Extremely_irregular_heart_sounds.wav.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="194112" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/7d/Extremely_irregular_heart_sounds.wav/Extremely_irregular_heart_sounds.wav.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><source data-bandwidth="1536000" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="WAV source" data-title="Original WAV file (1.54 Mbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7d/Extremely_irregular_heart_sounds.wav" type="audio/wav"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">Recording of heart sounds from a 16-year-old girl with a cardiac arrhythmia.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>While in the healthy heart, waves of electrical impulses originate in the <a href="/wiki/Sinoatrial_node" title="Sinoatrial node">sinus node</a> before spreading to the rest of the atria, the <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_node" title="Atrioventricular node">atrioventricular node</a>, and finally the ventricles (referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Sinus_rhythm" title="Sinus rhythm">normal sinus rhythm</a>), this normal rhythm can be disrupted. Abnormal heart rhythms or arrhythmias may be asymptomatic or may cause palpitations, blackouts, or breathlessness. Some types of arrhythmia such as <a href="/wiki/Atrial_fibrillation" title="Atrial fibrillation">atrial fibrillation</a> increase the long term risk of <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-68">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some arrhythmias cause the heart to beat abnormally slowly, referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Bradycardia" title="Bradycardia">bradycardia</a> or bradyarrhythmia. This may be caused by an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sick_sinus_syndrome" title="Sick sinus syndrome">abnormally slow sinus node</a> or damage within the cardiac conduction system (<a href="/wiki/Heart_block" title="Heart block">heart block</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-69">[62]</a></sup> In other arrhythmias the heart may beat abnormally rapidly, referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Tachycardia" title="Tachycardia">tachycardia</a> or tachyarrhythmia. These arrhythmias can take many forms and can originate from different structures within the heart—some arise from the atria (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Atrial_flutter" title="Atrial flutter">atrial flutter</a>), some from the atrioventricular node (e.g. <a href="/wiki/AV_nodal_reentrant_tachycardia" title="AV nodal reentrant tachycardia">AV nodal re-entrant tachycardia</a>) whilst others arise from the ventricles (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ventricular_tachycardia" title="Ventricular tachycardia">ventricular tachycardia</a>). Some tachyarrhythmias are caused by scarring within the heart (e.g. some forms of <a href="/wiki/Ventricular_tachycardia" title="Ventricular tachycardia">ventricular tachycardia</a>), others by an irritable focus (e.g. focal <a href="/wiki/Atrial_tachycardia" title="Atrial tachycardia">atrial tachycardia</a>), while others are caused by additional abnormal conduction tissue that has been present since birth (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Wolff%E2%80%93Parkinson%E2%80%93White_syndrome" title="Wolff–Parkinson–White syndrome">Wolff-Parkinson-White syndrome</a>). The most dangerous form of heart racing is <a href="/wiki/Ventricular_fibrillation" title="Ventricular fibrillation">ventricular fibrillation</a>, in which the ventricles quiver rather than contract, and which if untreated is rapidly fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pericardial_disease">Pericardial disease</span></h4>
<p>The sac which surrounds the heart, called the pericardium, can become inflamed in a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Pericarditis" title="Pericarditis">pericarditis</a>. This condition typically causes chest pain that may spread to the back, and is often caused by a viral infection (<a href="/wiki/Infectious_mononucleosis" title="Infectious mononucleosis">glandular fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytomegalovirus" title="Cytomegalovirus">cytomegalovirus</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Coxsackievirus" title="Coxsackievirus">coxsackievirus</a>). Fluid can build up within the pericardial sac, referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Pericardial_effusion" title="Pericardial effusion">pericardial effusion</a>. Pericardial effusions often occur secondary to pericarditis, kidney failure, or tumours, and frequently do not cause any symptoms. However, large effusions or effusions which accumulate rapidly can compress the heart in a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_tamponade" title="Cardiac tamponade">cardiac tamponade</a>, causing breathlessness and potentially fatal low blood pressure. Fluid can be removed from the pericardial space for diagnosis or to relieve tamponade using a syringe in a procedure called <a href="/wiki/Pericardiocentesis" title="Pericardiocentesis">pericardiocentesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010638–639_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010638–639-71">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Congenital_heart_disease">Congenital heart disease</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Congenital_heart_defect" title="Congenital heart defect">Congenital heart defect</a></div>
<p>Some people are born with hearts that are abnormal and these abnormalities are known as congenital heart defects. They may range from the relatively minor (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patent_foramen_ovale" title="Patent foramen ovale">patent foramen ovale</a>, arguably a variant of normal) to serious life-threatening abnormalities (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Hypoplastic_left_heart_syndrome" title="Hypoplastic left heart syndrome">hypoplastic left heart syndrome</a>). Common abnormalities include those that affect the heart muscle that separates the two side of the heart (a "hole in the heart", e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ventricular_septal_defect" title="Ventricular septal defect">ventricular septal defect</a>). Other defects include those affecting the heart valves (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Aortic_stenosis" title="Aortic stenosis">congenital aortic stenosis</a>), or the main blood vessels that lead from the heart (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Coarctation_of_the_aorta" title="Coarctation of the aorta">coarctation of the aorta</a>). More complex syndromes are seen that affect more than one part of the heart (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Tetralogy_of_Fallot" title="Tetralogy of Fallot">Tetralogy of Fallot</a>).
</p><p>Some congenital heart defects allow blood that is low in oxygen that would normally be returned to the lungs to instead be pumped back to the rest of the body. These are known as <a href="/wiki/Cyanotic_heart_defect" title="Cyanotic heart defect">cyanotic congenital heart defects</a> and are often more serious. Major congenital heart defects are often picked up in childhood, shortly after birth, or even before a child is born (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Transposition_of_the_great_vessels" title="Transposition of the great vessels">transposition of the great arteries</a>), causing breathlessness and a lower rate of growth. More minor forms of congenital heart disease may remain undetected for many years and only reveal themselves in adult life (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Atrial_septal_defect" title="Atrial septal defect">atrial septal defect</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison's20111458–65_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison's20111458–65-73">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h3>
<p>Heart disease is diagnosed by the taking of a <a href="/wiki/Medical_history" title="Medical history">medical history</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_examination" title="Cardiac examination">cardiac examination</a>, and further investigations, including <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood tests</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echocardiogram" title="Echocardiogram">echocardiograms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrocardiograms" title="Electrocardiograms">electrocardiograms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_imaging" title="Cardiac imaging">imaging</a>. Other invasive procedures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac_catheterisation" title="Cardiac catheterisation">cardiac catheterisation</a> can also play a role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010527–534_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010527–534-74">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Examination">Examination</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_examination" title="Cardiac examination">Cardiac examination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heart_sounds" title="Heart sounds">Heart sounds</a></div>
<p>The cardiac examination includes inspection, feeling the chest with the hands (<a href="/wiki/Palpation" title="Palpation">palpation</a>) and listening with a stethoscope (<a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">auscultation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidsons2010_1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidsons2010_1-75">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010522–536_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010522–536-76">[69]</a></sup> It involves assessment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_sign" title="Medical sign">signs</a> that may be visible on a person's hands (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Splinter_haemorrhage" title="Splinter haemorrhage">splinter haemorrhages</a>), joints and other areas. A person's pulse is taken, usually at the <a href="/wiki/Radial_artery" title="Radial artery">radial artery</a> near the wrist, in order to assess for the rhythm and strength of the pulse. The <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> is taken, using either a manual or automatic <a href="/wiki/Sphygmomanometer" title="Sphygmomanometer">sphygmomanometer</a> or using <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure#Invasive" title="Blood pressure">a more invasive measurement</a> from within the artery. Any elevation of the <a href="/wiki/Jugular_vein" title="Jugular vein">jugular venous pulse</a> is noted. A person's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest" title="Chest">chest</a> is felt for any transmitted vibrations from the heart, and then listened to with a stethoscope.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_sounds">Heart sounds</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apikal4D.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="636" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Apikal4D.gif/220px-Apikal4D.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Apikal4D.gif/330px-Apikal4D.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Apikal4D.gif/440px-Apikal4D.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apikal4D.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Echocardiography#Three-dimensional_echocardiography" title="Echocardiography">3D echocardiogram</a> showing the mitral valve (right), tricuspid and mitral valves (top left) and aortic valve (top right).<br/> The closure of the heart valves causes the <a href="/wiki/Heart_sounds" title="Heart sounds">heart sounds</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:HROgg.ogg" title="File:HROgg.ogg">Normal heart sounds</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="20" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="HROgg.ogg" id="mwe_player_8" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="30863" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (31 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/HROgg.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="40768" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/72/HROgg.ogg/HROgg.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">Normal heart sounds as heard with a <a href="/wiki/Stethoscope" title="Stethoscope">stethoscope</a></div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Typically, healthy hearts have only two audible <a href="/wiki/Heart_sounds" title="Heart sounds">heart sounds</a>, called S1 and S2. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_heart_sound" title="First heart sound">first heart sound</a> S1, is the sound created by the closing of the atrioventricular valves during ventricular contraction and is normally described as "lub". The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_heart_sound" title="Second heart sound">second heart sound</a>, S2, is the sound of the semilunar valves closing during ventricular diastole and is described as "dub".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-63"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> Each sound consists of two components, reflecting the slight difference in time as the two valves close.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TC2014_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-TC2014-77">[70]</a></sup> S2 may <a href="/wiki/Split_S2" title="Split S2">split</a> into two distinct sounds, either as a result of inspiration or different valvular or cardiac problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TC2014_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-TC2014-77">[70]</a></sup> Additional heart sounds may also be present and these give rise to <a href="/wiki/Gallop_rhythm" title="Gallop rhythm">gallop rhythms</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Third_heart_sound" title="Third heart sound">third heart sound</a>, S3 usually indicates an increase in ventricular blood volume. A <a href="/wiki/Fourth_heart_sound" title="Fourth heart sound">fourth heart sound</a> S4 is referred to as an atrial gallop and is produced by the sound of blood being forced into a stiff ventricle. The combined presence of S3 and S4 give a quadruple gallop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-64"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heart_murmur" title="Heart murmur">Heart murmurs</a> are abnormal heart sounds which can be either related to disease or benign, and there are several kinds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010556–559_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010556–559-78">[71]</a></sup> There are normally two heart sounds, and abnormal heart sounds can either be extra sounds, or "murmurs" related to the flow of blood between the sounds. Murmurs are graded by volume, from 1 (the quietest), to 6 (the loudest), and evaluated by their relationship to the heart sounds, position in the cardiac cycle, and additional features such as their radiation to other sites, changes with a person's position, the frequency of the sound as determined by the side of the <a href="/wiki/Stethoscope" title="Stethoscope">stethoscope</a> by which they are heard, and site at which they are heard loudest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010556–559_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010556–559-78">[71]</a></sup> Murmurs may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Valvular_heart_disease" title="Valvular heart disease">damaged heart valves</a> or congenital heart disease such as <a href="/wiki/Ventricular_septal_defect" title="Ventricular septal defect">ventricular septal defects</a>, or may be heard in normal hearts. A different type of sound, a <a href="/wiki/Pericardial_friction_rub" title="Pericardial friction rub">pericardial friction rub</a> can be heard in cases of pericarditis where the inflamed membranes can rub together.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_tests">Blood tests</span></h4>
<p>Blood tests play an important role in the diagnosis and treatment of many cardiovascular conditions.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Troponin" title="Troponin">Troponin</a> is a sensitive <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">biomarker</a> for a heart with insufficient blood supply. It is released 4–6 hours after injury, and usually peaks at about 12–24 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OH2010_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-OH2010-48">[41]</a></sup> Two tests of troponin are often taken—one at the time of initial presentation, and another within 3–6 hours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[72]</a></sup> with either a high level or a significant rise being diagnostic. A test for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_natriuretic_peptide" title="Brain natriuretic peptide">brain natriuretic peptide</a> (BNP) can be used to evaluate for the presence of heart failure, and rises when there is increased demand on the left ventricle. These tests are considered <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">biomarkers</a> because they are highly specific for cardiac disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010531_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010531-80">[73]</a></sup> Testing for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CK-MB" title="CK-MB">MB form of creatine kinase</a> provides information about the heart's blood supply, but is used less frequently because it is less specific and sensitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison's20111534_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison's20111534-81">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other blood tests are often taken to help understand a person's general health and risk factors that may contribute to heart disease. These often include a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_blood_count" title="Full blood count">full blood count</a> investigating for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaemia" title="Anaemia">anaemia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Basic_metabolic_panel" title="Basic metabolic panel">basic metabolic panel</a> that may reveal any disturbances in electrolytes. A <a href="/wiki/Coagulation_screen" title="Coagulation screen">coagulation screen</a> is often required to ensure that the right level of anticoagulation is given. <a href="/wiki/Lipid_profile" title="Lipid profile">Fasting lipids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasting_blood_glucose" title="Fasting blood glucose">fasting blood glucose</a> (or an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HbA1c" title="HbA1c">HbA1c</a> level) are often ordered to evaluate a person's <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> and diabetes status, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010521–640_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010521–640-82">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrocardiogram">Electrocardiogram</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electrocardiography" title="Electrocardiography">Electrocardiography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1683" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg/220px-2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg/330px-2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg/440px-2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2028_Cardiac_Cycle_vs_Electrocardiogram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cardiac cycle shown against ECG</div></div></div>
<p>Using surface electrodes on the body, it is possible to record the electrical activity of the heart. This tracing of the electrical signal is the electrocardiogram (ECG) or (EKG). An ECG is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bedside_test" title="Bedside test">bedside test</a> and involves the placement of ten leads on the body. This produces a "12 lead" ECG (three extra leads are calculated mathematically, and one lead is <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">electrically ground</a>, or earthed).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530-83">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are five prominent features on the ECG: the <a href="/wiki/P_wave_(electrocardiography)" title="P wave (electrocardiography)">P wave</a> (atrial depolarisation), the <a href="/wiki/QRS_complex" title="QRS complex">QRS complex</a> (ventricular depolarisation)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[h]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/T_wave" title="T wave">T wave</a> (ventricular repolarisation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-65"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup> As the heart cells contract, they create a current that travels through the heart. A downward deflection on the ECG implies cells are becoming more positive in charge ("depolarising") in the direction of that lead, whereas an upward inflection implies cells are becoming more negative ("repolarising") in the direction of the lead. This depends on the position of the lead, so if a wave of depolarising moved from left to right, a lead on the left would show a negative deflection, and a lead on the right would show a positive deflection. The ECG is a useful tool in detecting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrythmia" title="Arrythmia">rhythm disturbances</a> and in detecting insufficient blood supply to the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530-83">[76]</a></sup> Sometimes abnormalities are suspected, but not immediately visible on the ECG. <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_stress_test" title="Cardiac stress test">Testing when exercising</a> can be used to provoke an abnormality, or an ECG can be worn for a longer period such as a 24-hour <a href="/wiki/Holter_monitor" title="Holter monitor">Holter monitor</a> if a suspected rhythm abnormality is not present at the time of assessment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530-83">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Imaging">Imaging</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_imaging" title="Cardiac imaging">Cardiac imaging</a></div>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">imaging</a> methods can be used to assess the anatomy and function of the heart, including <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> (<a href="/wiki/Echocardiography" title="Echocardiography">echocardiography</a>), <a href="/wiki/Angiography" title="Angiography">angiography</a>, <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRI</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">PET, scans</a>. An echocardiogram is an ultrasound of the heart used to measure the heart's function, assess for valve disease, and look for any abnormalities. Echocardiography can be conducted by a probe on the chest (<a href="/wiki/Transthoracic_echocardiogram" title="Transthoracic echocardiogram">transthoracic</a>), or by a probe in the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Transesophageal_echocardiogram" title="Transesophageal echocardiogram">transesophageal</a>). A typical echocardiography report will include information about the width of the valves noting any <a href="/wiki/Stenosis" title="Stenosis">stenosis</a>, whether there is any backflow of blood (<a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(circulation)" title="Regurgitation (circulation)">regurgitation</a>) and information about the blood volumes at the end of systole and diastole, including an <a href="/wiki/Ejection_fraction" title="Ejection fraction">ejection fraction</a>, which describes how much blood is ejected from the left and right ventricles after systole. Ejection fraction can then be obtained by dividing the volume ejected by the heart (stroke volume) by the volume of the filled heart (end-diastolic volume).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup> Echocardiograms can also be conducted under circumstances when the body is more stressed, in order to examine for signs of lack of blood supply. This <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_stress_test" title="Cardiac stress test">cardiac stress test</a> involves either direct exercise, or where this is not possible, injection of a drug such as <a href="/wiki/Dobutamine" title="Dobutamine">dobutamine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010522–536_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010522–536-76">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>CT scans, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_X-rays" title="Chest X-rays">chest X-rays</a> and other forms of imaging can help evaluate the heart's size, evaluate for signs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_oedema" title="Pulmonary oedema">pulmonary oedema</a>, and indicate whether there is <a href="/wiki/Pericardial_effusion" title="Pericardial effusion">fluid around the heart</a>. They are also useful for evaluating the aorta, the major blood vessel which leaves the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010522–536_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010522–536-76">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h3>
<p>Diseases affecting the heart can be treated by a variety of methods including lifestyle modification, drug treatment, and surgery.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ischaemic_heart_disease_2">Ischaemic heart disease</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">Coronary artery disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_bypass_surgery" title="Coronary artery bypass surgery">Coronary artery bypass surgery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coronary_stent" title="Coronary stent">Coronary stent</a></div><p>Narrowings of the coronary arteries (ischaemic heart disease) are treated to relieve symptoms of chest pain caused by a partially narrowed artery (angina pectoris), to minimise heart muscle damage when an artery is completely occluded (<a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a>), or to prevent a myocardial infarction from occurring. Medications to improve angina symptoms include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_use_of_nitroglycerin" title="Medical use of nitroglycerin">nitroglycerin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beta_blocker" title="Beta blocker">beta blockers</a>, and calcium channel blockers, while preventative treatments include <a href="/wiki/Antiplatelet_drug" title="Antiplatelet drug">antiplatelets</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statin" title="Statin">statins</a>, lifestyle measures such as stopping smoking and weight loss, and treatment of risk factors such as high blood pressure and diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to using medications, narrowed heart arteries can be treated by expanding the narrowings or redirecting the flow of blood to bypass an obstruction. This may be performed using a <a href="/wiki/Percutaneous_coronary_intervention" title="Percutaneous coronary intervention">percutaneous coronary intervention</a>, during which narrowings can be expanded by passing small balloon-tipped wires into the coronary arteries, inflating the balloon to expand the narrowing, and sometimes leaving behind a metal scaffold known as a stent to keep the artery open.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-87">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>If the narrowings in coronary arteries are unsuitable for treatment with a percutaneous coronary intervention, open surgery may be required. A <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_bypass_surgery" title="Coronary artery bypass surgery">coronary artery bypass graft</a> can be performed, whereby a blood vessel from another part of the body (the <a href="/wiki/Great_saphenous_vein" title="Great saphenous vein">saphenous vein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radial_artery" title="Radial artery">radial artery</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Internal_thoracic_artery" title="Internal thoracic artery">internal mammary artery</a>) is used to redirect blood from a point before the narrowing (typically the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a>) to a point beyond the obstruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-87">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010585–588,_614–623_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010585–588,_614–623-88">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Valvular_heart_disease_2">Valvular heart disease</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artificial_heart_valve" title="Artificial heart valve">Artificial heart valve</a></div><p>Diseased heart valves that have become abnormally narrow or abnormally leaky may require surgery. This is traditionally performed as an open surgical procedure to replace the damaged heart valve with a tissue or metallic <a href="/wiki/Artificial_heart_valve" title="Artificial heart valve">prosthetic valve</a>.
