In some circumstances, the <a href="/wiki/Tricuspid_valve" title="Tricuspid valve">tricuspid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mitral_valve" title="Mitral valve">mitral</a> valves can be repaired <a href="/wiki/Mitral_valve_repair" title="Mitral valve repair">surgically</a>, avoiding the need for a valve replacement. Heart valves can also be treated percutaneously, using techniques that share many similarities with percutaneous coronary intervention. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcatheter_Aortic_Valve_Replacement" title="Transcatheter Aortic Valve Replacement">Transcatheter aortic valve replacement</a> is increasingly used for patients consider very high risk for open valve replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-66">[59]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiac_arrhythmias_2">Cardiac arrhythmias</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_arrhythmia" title="Heart arrhythmia">Heart arrhythmia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiofrequency_ablation" title="Radiofrequency ablation">Radiofrequency ablation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_cardiac_pacemaker" title="Artificial cardiac pacemaker">Artificial cardiac pacemaker</a></div><p>Abnormal heart rhythms (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_arrhythmia" title="Heart arrhythmia">arrhythmias</a>) can be treated using antiarrhythmic drugs. These may work by manipulating the flow of electrolytes across the cell membrane (such as <a href="/wiki/Calcium_channel_blocker" title="Calcium channel blocker">calcium channel blockers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_channel_blocker" title="Sodium channel blocker">sodium channel blockers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amiodarone" title="Amiodarone">amiodarone</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Digoxin" title="Digoxin">digoxin</a>), or modify the autonomic nervous system's effect on the heart (<a href="/wiki/Beta_blocker" title="Beta blocker">beta blockers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atropine" title="Atropine">atropine</a>). In some arrhythmias such as atrial fibrillation which increase the risk of stroke, this risk can be reduced using anticoagulants such as <a href="/wiki/Warfarin" title="Warfarin">warfarin</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novel_oral_anticoagulants" title="Novel oral anticoagulants">novel oral anticoagulants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-68">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>If medications fail to control an arrhythmia, another treatment option may be <a href="/wiki/Catheter_ablation" title="Catheter ablation">catheter ablation</a>. In these procedures, wires are passed from a <a href="/wiki/Femoral_vein" title="Femoral vein">vein</a> or <a href="/wiki/Femoral_artery" title="Femoral artery">artery</a> in the leg to the heart to find the abnormal area of tissue that is causing the arrhythmia. The abnormal tissue can be intentionally damaged, or ablated, by <a href="/wiki/Radiofrequency_ablation" title="Radiofrequency ablation">heating</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cryoablation" title="Cryoablation">freezing</a> to prevent further heart rhythm disturbances. Whilst the majority of arrhythmias can be treated using minimally invasive catheter techniques, some arrhythmias (particularly <a href="/wiki/Atrial_fibrillation" title="Atrial fibrillation">atrial fibrillation</a>) can also be treated using open or <a href="/wiki/Thoracoscopy" title="Thoracoscopy">thoracoscopic</a> surgery, either at the time of other cardiac surgery or as a standalone procedure. A <a href="/wiki/Cardioversion" title="Cardioversion">cardioversion</a>, whereby an electric shock is used to stun the heart out of an abnormal rhythm, may also be used.
</p><p>Cardiac devices in the form of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_cardiac_pacemaker" title="Artificial cardiac pacemaker">pacemakers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Defibrillation" title="Defibrillation">implantable defibrillators</a> may also be required to treat arrhythmias. Pacemakers, comprising a small battery powered generator implanted under the skin and one or more leads that extend to the heart, are most commonly used to treat abnormally <a href="/wiki/Bradycardia" title="Bradycardia">slow heart rhythms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-69">[62]</a></sup> Implantable defibrillators are used to treat serious life-threatening rapid heart rhythms. These devices monitor the heart, and if dangerous heart racing is detected can automatically deliver a shock to restore the heart to a normal rhythm. Implantable defibrillators are most commonly used in patients with heart failure, <a href="/wiki/Cardiomyopathy" title="Cardiomyopathy">cardiomyopathies</a>, or inherited arrhythmia syndromes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_failure_2">Heart failure</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">Heart failure</a></div><p>As well as addressing the underlying cause for a patient's heart failure (most commonly <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">ischaemic heart disease</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>), the mainstay of heart failure treatment is with medication. These include drugs to prevent fluid from accumulating in the lungs by increasing the amount of urine a patient produces (<a href="/wiki/Diuretic" title="Diuretic">diuretics</a>), and drugs that attempt to preserve the pumping function of the heart (<a href="/wiki/Beta_blocker" title="Beta blocker">beta blockers</a>, <a href="/wiki/ACE_inhibitor" title="ACE inhibitor">ACE inhibitors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimineralocorticoid" title="Antimineralocorticoid">mineralocorticoid receptor antagonists</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-65">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some patients with heart failure, a specialised pacemaker known as <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_resynchronization_therapy" title="Cardiac resynchronization therapy">cardiac resynchronisation therapy</a> can be used to improve the heart's pumping efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-69">[62]</a></sup> These devices are frequently combined with a defibrillator. In very severe cases of heart failure, a small pump called a <a href="/wiki/Ventricular_assist_device" title="Ventricular assist device">ventricular assist device</a> may be implanted which supplements the heart's own pumping ability. In the most severe cases, a <a href="/wiki/Heart_transplantation" title="Heart transplantation">cardiac transplant</a> may be considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-65">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_da_vinci,_Heart_and_its_Blood_Vessels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="365" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Leonardo_da_vinci%2C_Heart_and_its_Blood_Vessels.jpg/220px-Leonardo_da_vinci%2C_Heart_and_its_Blood_Vessels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Leonardo_da_vinci%2C_Heart_and_its_Blood_Vessels.jpg/330px-Leonardo_da_vinci%2C_Heart_and_its_Blood_Vessels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/Leonardo_da_vinci%2C_Heart_and_its_Blood_Vessels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_da_vinci,_Heart_and_its_Blood_Vessels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heart and its blood vessels, by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, 15th century</div></div></div>
<p>Humans have known about the heart since ancient times, although its precise function and anatomy were not clearly understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USYD2016_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-USYD2016-89">[81]</a></sup> From the primarily religious views of earlier societies towards the heart, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> are considered to have been the primary seat of scientific understanding of the heart in the ancient world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anaemia2010_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anaemia2010-90">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katz2008_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2008-91">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aird2011_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aird2011-92">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> considered the heart to be the organ responsible for creating blood; <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> considered the heart as the source of circulating blood and <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> noted blood circulating cyclically from the body through the heart to the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anaemia2010_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anaemia2010-90">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aird2011_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aird2011-92">[84]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erasistratos" title="Erasistratos">Erasistratos</a> (304–250 BCE) noted the heart as a pump, causing dilation of blood vessels, and noted that arteries and veins both radiate from the heart, becoming progressively smaller with distance, although he believed they were filled with air and not blood. He also discovered the heart valves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anaemia2010_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Anaemia2010-90">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> (2nd century CE) knew blood vessels carried blood and identified venous (dark red) and arterial (brighter and thinner) blood, each with distinct and separate functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anaemia2010_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-Anaemia2010-90">[82]</a></sup> Galen, noting the heart as the hottest organ in the body, concluded that it provided heat to the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aird2011_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aird2011-92">[84]</a></sup> The heart did not pump blood around, the heart's motion sucked blood in during diastole and the blood moved by the pulsation of the arteries themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aird2011_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-Aird2011-92">[84]</a></sup> Galen believed the arterial blood was created by venous blood passing from the left ventricle to the right through 'pores' between the ventricles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USYD2016_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-USYD2016-89">[81]</a></sup> Air from the lungs passed from the lungs via the pulmonary artery to the left side of the heart and created arterial blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aird2011_92-4"><a href="#cite_note-Aird2011-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>These ideas went unchallenged for almost a thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USYD2016_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-USYD2016-89">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aird2011_92-5"><a href="#cite_note-Aird2011-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-modern">Pre-modern</span></h3>
<p>The earliest descriptions of the <a href="/wiki/Coronary_circulation" title="Coronary circulation">coronary</a> and pulmonary circulation systems can be found in the <i><a href="/wiki/Commentary_on_Anatomy_in_Avicenna%27s_Canon" title="Commentary on Anatomy in Avicenna's Canon">Commentary on Anatomy in Avicenna's Canon</a></i>, published in 1242 by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michelakis_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michelakis-93">[85]</a></sup> In his manuscript, al-Nafis wrote that blood passes through the pulmonary circulation instead of moving from the right to the left ventricle as previously believed by Galen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West2008_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-West2008-94">[86]</a></sup> His work was later translated into <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> by <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Alpago" title="Andrea Alpago">Andrea Alpago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Europe, the teachings of Galen continued to dominate the academic community and his doctrines were adopted as the official canon of the Church. <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a> questioned some of Galen's beliefs of the heart in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_humani_corporis_fabrica" title="De humani corporis fabrica">De humani corporis fabrica</a></i> (1543), but his <a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">magnum opus</a> was interpreted as a challenge to the authorities and he was subjected to a number of attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-west2014_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-west2014-96">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Servetus" title="Michael Servetus">Michael Servetus</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/Christianismi_Restitutio" title="Christianismi Restitutio">Christianismi Restitutio</a></i> (1553) that blood flows from one side of the heart to the other via the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-west2014_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-west2014-96">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif/220px-Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif/330px-Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif/440px-Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animated_Heart_-_Old_Textbook_style.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animated heart</div></div></div>
<p>A breakthrough in understanding the flow of blood through the heart and body came with the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Exercitatio_Anatomica_de_Motu_Cordis_et_Sanguinis_in_Animalibus" title="Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus">De Motu Cordis</a></i> (1628) by the English physician <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>. Harvey's book completely describes the systemic circulation and the mechanical force of the heart, leading to an overhaul of the Galenic doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aird2011_92-6"><a href="#cite_note-Aird2011-92">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Otto_Frank_(physiologist)" title="Otto Frank (physiologist)">Otto Frank</a> (1865–1944) was a German physiologist; among his many published works are detailed studies of this important heart relationship. <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Starling" title="Ernest Starling">Ernest Starling</a> (1866–1927) was an important English physiologist who also studied the heart. Although they worked largely independently, their combined efforts and similar conclusions have been recognized in the name "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank%E2%80%93Starling_mechanism" title="Frank–Starling mechanism">Frank–Starling mechanism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNX2014_13-66"><a href="#cite_note-CNX2014-13">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Purkinje_fibers" title="Purkinje fibers">Purkinje fibers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bundle_of_His" title="Bundle of His">bundle of His</a> were discovered as early as the 19th century, their specific role in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conduction_system_of_the_heart" title="Electrical conduction system of the heart">electrical conduction system of the heart</a> remained unknown until <a href="/wiki/Sunao_Tawara" title="Sunao Tawara">Sunao Tawara</a> published his monograph, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Reizleitungssystem_des_S%C3%A4ugetierherzens" title="Das Reizleitungssystem des Säugetierherzens">Das Reizleitungssystem des Säugetierherzens</a></i>, in 1906. Tawara's discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_node" title="Atrioventricular node">atrioventricular node</a> prompted <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Keith" title="Arthur Keith">Arthur Keith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Flack" title="Martin Flack">Martin Flack</a> to look for similar structures in the heart, leading to their discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Sinoatrial_node" title="Sinoatrial node">sinoatrial node</a> several months later. These structures form the anatomical basis of the electrocardiogram, whose inventor, <a href="/wiki/Willem_Einthoven" title="Willem Einthoven">Willem Einthoven</a>, was awarded the Nobel Prize in Medicine or Physiology in 1924.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Heart_transplantation" title="Heart transplantation">heart transplant</a> in a human ever performed was by <a href="/wiki/James_Hardy_(surgeon)" title="James Hardy (surgeon)">James Hardy</a> in 1964, using a chimpanzee heart, but the patient died within 2 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup> The first human to human heart transplantation was performed in 1967 by the South African surgeon <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Barnard" title="Christiaan Barnard">Christiaan Barnard</a> at <a href="/wiki/Groote_Schuur_Hospital" title="Groote Schuur Hospital">Groote Schuur Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup> This marked an important milestone in <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_surgery" title="Cardiac surgery">cardiac surgery</a>, capturing the attention of both the medical profession and the world at large. However, long-term survival rates of patients were initially very low. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Washkansky" title="Louis Washkansky">Louis Washkansky</a>, the first recipient of a donated heart, died 18 days after the operation while other patients did not survive for more than a few weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup> The American surgeon <a href="/wiki/Norman_Shumway" title="Norman Shumway">Norman Shumway</a> has been credited for his efforts to improve transplantation techniques, along with pioneers <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lower_(surgeon)" title="Richard Lower (surgeon)">Richard Lower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Demikhov" title="Vladimir Demikhov">Vladimir Demikhov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Kantrowitz" title="Adrian Kantrowitz">Adrian Kantrowitz</a>. As of March 2000, more than 55,000 heart transplantations have been performed worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[94]</a></sup> The first successful transplant of a heart from a <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified</a> pig to a human in which the patient lived for a longer time, was performed January 7, 2022 in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a> by heart surgeon <a href="/wiki/Bartley_P._Griffith" title="Bartley P. Griffith">Bartley P. Griffith</a>, recipient was David Bennett (57) this successfully extended his life until 8 March 2022 (1 month and 30 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the middle of the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">heart disease</a> had surpassed infectious disease as the leading cause of death in the United States, and it is currently the leading cause of deaths worldwide. Since 1948, the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Framingham_Heart_Study" title="Framingham Heart Study">Framingham Heart Study</a> has shed light on the effects of various influences on the heart, including diet, exercise, and common medications such as aspirin. Although the introduction of <a href="/wiki/ACE_inhibitor" title="ACE inhibitor">ACE inhibitors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_blocker" title="Beta blocker">beta blockers</a> has improved the management of chronic heart failure, the disease continues to be an enormous medical and societal burden, with 30 to 40% of patients dying within a year of receiving the diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Heart" title="Sacred Heart">Sacred Heart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart_symbol" title="Heart symbol">Heart symbol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blood#Cultural_and_religious_beliefs" title="Blood">Blood § Cultural and religious beliefs</a></div>
<table style="font-size: 88%; border-collapse: collapse; border: 1px solid #ddd; margin: 0.5em 1em; text-align: center; clear: right; float: right; margin-right: 0;">

<td style="padding:0.75em; background-color: white"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><table class="mw-hiero-table mw-hiero-outer" dir="ltr"><tbody><tr><td> <table class="mw-hiero-table"><tbody><tr> <td><img alt="F34" height="17" src="/w/extensions/wikihiero/img/hiero_F34.png?6be6f" style="margin: 1px;" title="F34"/></td></tr></tbody></table> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th style="background-color:#ccc"><i>jb</i> (F34) "heart"
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolism">Symbolism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heart_coraz%C3%B3n.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="130" data-file-width="130" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Heart_coraz%C3%B3n.svg/100px-Heart_coraz%C3%B3n.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Heart_coraz%C3%B3n.svg/150px-Heart_coraz%C3%B3n.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Heart_coraz%C3%B3n.svg/200px-Heart_coraz%C3%B3n.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_(symbol)" title="Heart (symbol)">heart symbol</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%93%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="175" data-file-width="166" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/%D0%93%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8.png/100px-%D0%93%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/%D0%93%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8.png/150px-%D0%93%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/%D0%93%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8.png 2x" width="100"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Letter <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%83%A6" title="wikt:ღ">ღ</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgian_script" title="Georgian script">Georgian script</a> is often used as a "heart" symbol.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%BF%83-bigseal.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/%E5%BF%83-bigseal.svg/100px-%E5%BF%83-bigseal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/%E5%BF%83-bigseal.svg/150px-%E5%BF%83-bigseal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/%E5%BF%83-bigseal.svg/200px-%E5%BF%83-bigseal.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a> glyph for "heart" (<a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> <i>sim</i>)</div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5981" data-file-width="4480" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg/220px-Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg/330px-Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg/440px-Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amaranthe_-_Wacken_Open_Air_2018-2480.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Elize_Ryd" title="Elize Ryd">Elize Ryd</a> making a heart sign at a concert in 2018</div></div></div>
<p>As one of the vital organs, the heart was long identified as the center of the entire body, the seat of life, or emotion, or reason, will, intellect, purpose or the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WATKINS_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-WATKINS-105">[97]</a></sup> The heart is an emblematic symbol in many religions, signifying "truth, conscience or moral courage in many religions—the temple or throne of God in Islamic and <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> thought; the divine centre, or <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">atman</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Third_eye" title="Third eye">third eye</a> of transcendent wisdom in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>; the diamond of purity and essence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoist</a> centre of understanding."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WATKINS_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-WATKINS-105">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, the word for heart, <i>lev</i>, is used in these meanings, as the seat of emotion, the mind, and referring to the anatomical organ. It is also connected in function and symbolism to the stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important part of the concept of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul" title="Egyptian soul">soul</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">Ancient Egyptian religion</a> was thought to be the heart, or <i>ib</i>. The <i>ib</i> or metaphysical heart was believed to be formed from one drop of blood from the child's mother's heart, taken at conception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[99]</a></sup> To ancient Egyptians, the heart was the seat of <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thought</a>, will, and <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intention</a>. This is evidenced by <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> expressions which incorporate the word <i>ib</i>, such as <i>Awi-ib</i> for "happy" (literally, "long of heart"), <i>Xak-ib</i> for "estranged" (literally, "truncated of heart").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup> In Egyptian religion, the heart was the key to the afterlife. It was conceived as surviving death in the nether world, where it gave evidence for, or against, its possessor. It was thought that the heart was examined by <a href="/wiki/Anubis" title="Anubis">Anubis</a> and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_deities" title="Ancient Egyptian deities">deities</a> during the <i>Weighing of the Heart</i> ceremony. If the heart weighed more than the feather of <a href="/wiki/Maat" title="Maat">Maat</a>, which symbolized the ideal standard of behavior. If the scales balanced, it meant the heart's possessor had lived a just life and could enter the afterlife; if the heart was heavier, it would be devoured by the monster <a href="/wiki/Ammit" title="Ammit">Ammit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> character for "heart", 心, derives from a comparatively realistic depiction of a heart (indicating the heart chambers) in <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[102]</a></sup> The Chinese word <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BF%83#Mandarin" title="wikt:心"><i>xīn</i></a> also takes the metaphorical meanings of "mind", "intention", or "core".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heart_(Chinese_medicine)" title="Heart (Chinese medicine)">In Chinese medicine</a>, the heart is seen as the center of <a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">神 <i>shén</i></a> "spirit, consciousness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup> The heart is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a>, governs the <a href="/wiki/Zang-fu" title="Zang-fu">six organs and five viscera</a>, and belongs to fire in the five elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sanskrit word for heart is <i>hṛd</i> or <i>hṛdaya</i>, found in the oldest surviving Sanskrit text, the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>. In Sanskrit, it may mean both the anatomical object and "mind" or "soul", representing the seat of emotion. <i>Hrd</i> may be a cognate of the word for heart in Greek, Latin, and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical</a> philosophers and scientists, including <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, considered the heart the seat of thought, <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, or emotion, often disregarding the brain as contributing to those functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[108]</a></sup> The identification of the heart as the seat of <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotions</a> in particular is due to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, who also located the seat of the passions in the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, and the seat of reason in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The heart also played a role in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> system of belief. The most common form of human sacrifice practiced by the Aztecs was heart-extraction. The Aztec believed that the heart (<i>tona</i>) was both the seat of the individual and a fragment of the Sun's heat (<i>istli</i>). To this day, the Nahua consider the Sun to be a heart-soul (<i>tona-tiuh</i>): "round, hot, pulsating".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a>, there has been a long tradition of veneration of the heart, stemming from worship of the wounds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> which gained prominence from the mid sixteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup> This tradition influenced the development of the medieval Christian <a href="/wiki/Catholic_devotions" title="Catholic devotions">devotion</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_Heart_of_Jesus" title="Sacred Heart of Jesus">Sacred Heart of Jesus</a> and the parallel veneration of the <a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Heart_of_Mary" title="Immaculate Heart of Mary">Immaculate Heart of Mary</a>, made popular by <a href="/wiki/John_Eudes" title="John Eudes">John Eudes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The expression of a <a href="/wiki/Broken_heart" title="Broken heart">broken heart</a> is a cross-cultural reference to <a href="/wiki/Grief" title="Grief">grief</a> for a lost one or to unfulfilled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantic_love" title="Romantic love">romantic love</a>.
</p><p>The notion of "<a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupid</a>'s arrows" is ancient, due to <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, but while Ovid describes Cupid as wounding his victims with his arrows, it is not made explicit that it is the <i>heart</i> that is wounded. The familiar iconography of Cupid shooting little <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_shape" title="Heart shape">heart symbols</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> theme that became tied to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_day" title="Valentine's day">Valentine's day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WATKINS_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-WATKINS-105">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Food"></span><span class="anchor" id="Heart_as_food"></span>
Animal hearts are widely consumed as food. As they are almost entirely muscle, they are high in protein. They are often included in dishes with other <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a>, for example in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine" title="Ottoman cuisine">pan-Ottoman</a> <a href="/wiki/Kokoretsi" title="Kokoretsi">kokoretsi</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_(food)" title="Chicken (food)">Chicken</a> hearts are considered to be <a href="/wiki/Giblets" title="Giblets">giblets</a>, and are often grilled on skewers; examples of this are <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Yakitori" title="Yakitori"><i>hāto yakitori</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_cuisine" title="Brazilian cuisine">Brazilian</a> <a href="/wiki/Churrasco" title="Churrasco"><i>churrasco de coração</i></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_cuisine" title="Indonesian cuisine">Indonesian</a> <a href="/wiki/Satay" title="Satay">chicken heart satay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[113]</a></sup> They can also be pan-fried, as in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_mixed_grill" title="Jerusalem mixed grill">Jerusalem mixed grill</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_cuisine" title="Egyptian cuisine">Egyptian cuisine</a>, they can be used, finely chopped, as part of <a href="/wiki/Stuffing" title="Stuffing">stuffing</a> for chicken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup> Many recipes combined them with other giblets, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_cuisine" title="Mexican cuisine">Mexican</a> <i>pollo en menudencias</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_cuisine" title="Russian cuisine">Russian</a> <i>ragu iz kurinyikh potrokhov</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hearts of beef, pork, and mutton can generally be interchanged in recipes. As heart is a hard-working muscle, it makes for "firm and rather dry" meat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup> so is generally slow-cooked. Another way of dealing with toughness is to <a href="/wiki/Julienning" title="Julienning">julienne</a> the meat, as in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cuisine" title="Chinese cuisine">Chinese</a> stir-fried heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">Beef</a> heart may be grilled or braised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joy_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-joy-127">[119]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_cuisine" title="Peruvian cuisine">Peruvian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticuchos" title="Anticuchos"><i>anticuchos de corazón</i></a>, barbecued beef hearts are grilled after being tenderized through long <a href="/wiki/Marination" title="Marination">marination</a> in a spice and vinegar mixture. An <a href="/wiki/Australian_cuisine" title="Australian cuisine">Australian</a> recipe for "mock goose" is actually braised stuffed beef heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">Pig</a> heart is stewed, poached, braised,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> or made into sausage. The <a href="/wiki/Balinese_cuisine" title="Balinese cuisine">Balinese</a> <i>oret</i> is a sort of <a href="/wiki/Blood_sausage" title="Blood sausage">blood sausage</a> made with pig heart and blood. A <a href="/wiki/French_cuisine" title="French cuisine">French</a> recipe for <i>cœur de porc à l'orange</i> is made of braised heart with an orange sauce.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">Circulatory system</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertebrates">Vertebrates</span></h3>
<p>The size of the heart varies among the different <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">groups</a>, with hearts in <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> ranging from those of the smallest mice (12 mg) to the blue whale (600 kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup> In vertebrates, the heart lies in the middle of the ventral part of the body, surrounded by a <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman1992_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman1992-131">[123]</a></sup> which in some fish may be connected to the <a href="/wiki/Peritoneum" title="Peritoneum">peritoneum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sinoatrial node is found in all <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a> but not in more primitive vertebrates. In these animals, the muscles of the heart are relatively continuous, and the sinus venosus coordinates the beat, which passes in a wave through the remaining chambers. Since the sinus venosus is incorporated into the right atrium in amniotes, it is likely <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> with the SA node. In teleosts, with their vestigial sinus venosus, the main centre of coordination is, instead, in the atrium. The rate of heartbeat varies enormously between different species, ranging from around 20 beats per minute in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codfish" title="Codfish">codfish</a> to around 600 in <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbirds</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-VB-133">[125]</a></sup> and up to 1200 bpm in the <a href="/wiki/Ruby-throated_hummingbird" title="Ruby-throated hummingbird">ruby-throated hummingbird</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Double_circulatory_systems">Double circulatory systems</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Reptile#Circulation" title="Reptile">Reptile § Circulation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Snake#Internal_organs" title="Snake">Snake § Internal organs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg/220px-Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg/330px-Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg/440px-Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copy_of_Ap_Bio_2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cross section of a three-chambered adult amphibian heart. Note the single ventricle. The purple regions represent areas where mixing of oxygenated and de-oxygenated blood occurs. <div><ol><li>Pulmonary vein</li><li>Left atrium</li><li>Right atrium</li><li>Ventricle</li><li>Conus arteriosus</li><li>Sinus venosus</li></ol></div></div></div></div>
<p>Adult <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> and most <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_circulatory_system" title="Double circulatory system">double circulatory system</a>, meaning a circulatory system divided into arterial and venous parts. However, the heart itself is not completely separated into two sides. Instead, it is separated into three chambers—two atria and one ventricle. Blood returning from both the systemic circulation and the lungs is returned, and blood is pumped simultaneously into the systemic circulation and the lungs. The double system allows blood to circulate to and from the lungs which deliver oxygenated blood directly to the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRIMM2015_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRIMM2015-135">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In reptiles, other than <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, the heart is usually situated around the middle of the thorax. In terrestrial and arboreal snakes it is usually located nearer to the head; in aquatic species the heart is more centrally located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-136">[128]</a></sup> There is a heart with three chambers: two atria and one ventricle. The form and function of these hearts are different than mammalian hearts due to the fact that snakes have an elongated body, and thus are affected by different environmental factors. In particular, the snake's heart relative to the position in their body has been influenced greatly by gravity. Therefore, snakes that are larger in size tend to have a higher <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> due to gravitational change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour-136">[128]</a></sup> The ventricle is incompletely separated into two-halves by a wall (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/septum" title="wikt:septum">septum</a>), with a considerable gap near the pulmonary artery and aortic openings. In most reptilian species, there appears to be little, if any, mixing between the bloodstreams, so the aorta receives, essentially, only oxygenated blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-VB-133">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRIMM2015_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRIMM2015-135">[127]</a></sup> The exception to this rule is <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>, which have a four-chambered heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COLVILLE2015_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-COLVILLE2015-137">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the heart of <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a>, the septum extends partway into the ventricle. This allows for some degree of separation between the de-oxygenated bloodstream destined for the lungs and the oxygenated stream that is delivered to the rest of the body. The absence of such a division in living amphibian species may be partly due to the amount of respiration that occurs through the skin; thus, the blood returned to the heart through the venae cavae is already partially oxygenated. As a result, there may be less need for a finer division between the two bloodstreams than in lungfish or other <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>. Nonetheless, in at least some species of amphibian, the spongy nature of the ventricle does seem to maintain more of a separation between the bloodstreams. Also, the original valves of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conus_arteriosus" title="Conus arteriosus">conus arteriosus</a> have been replaced by a spiral valve that divides it into two parallel parts, thereby helping to keep the two bloodstreams separate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-VB-133">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Full_division">Full division</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">Archosaurs</a> (<a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> show complete separation of the heart into two pumps for a total of four heart chambers; it is thought that the four-chambered heart of archosaurs evolved independently from that of mammals. In crocodilians, there is a small opening, the <a href="/wiki/Foramen_of_Panizza" title="Foramen of Panizza">foramen of Panizza</a>, at the base of the arterial trunks and there is some degree of mixing between the blood in each side of the heart, during a dive underwater;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nonimportantshunt1_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-nonimportantshunt1-138">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nonimportantshunt2_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-nonimportantshunt2-139">[131]</a></sup> thus, only in birds and mammals are the two streams of blood—those to the pulmonary and systemic circulations—permanently kept entirely separate by a physical barrier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_133-3"><a href="#cite_note-VB-133">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fish_anatomy#Heart" title="Fish anatomy">Fish anatomy § Heart</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_chamber_heart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Two_chamber_heart.svg/220px-Two_chamber_heart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Two_chamber_heart.svg/330px-Two_chamber_heart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Two_chamber_heart.svg/440px-Two_chamber_heart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_chamber_heart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Blood flow through the fish heart: sinus venosus, atrium, ventricle, and outflow tract</div></div></div></div>
<p>The heart evolved no less than 380 million years ago in fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380m_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-380m-140">[132]</a></sup> Fish have what is often described as a two-chambered heart,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jurd2004_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jurd2004-141">[133]</a></sup> consisting of one atrium to receive blood and one ventricle to pump it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2000_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostrander2000-142">[134]</a></sup> However, the fish heart has entry and exit compartments that may be called chambers, so it is also sometimes described as three-chambered<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrander2000_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ostrander2000-142">[134]</a></sup> or four-chambered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farrell2011_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farrell2011-143">[135]</a></sup> depending on what is counted as a chamber. The atrium and ventricle are sometimes considered "true chambers", while the others are considered "accessory chambers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shukla_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shukla-144">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Primitive fish have a four-chambered heart, but the chambers are arranged sequentially so that this primitive heart is quite unlike the four-chambered hearts of mammals and birds. The first chamber is the <a href="/wiki/Sinus_venosus" title="Sinus venosus">sinus venosus</a>, which collects deoxygenated blood from the body through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic_vein" title="Hepatic vein">hepatic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Cardinal_vein_(disambiguation)" title="Cardinal vein (disambiguation)">cardinal veins</a>. From here, blood flows into the atrium and then to the powerful muscular ventricle where the main pumping action will take place. The fourth and final chamber is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conus_arteriosus" title="Conus arteriosus">conus arteriosus</a>, which contains several valves and sends blood to the <i>ventral aorta</i>. The ventral aorta delivers blood to the gills where it is oxygenated and flows, through the <a href="/wiki/Descending_aorta" title="Descending aorta">dorsal aorta</a>, into the rest of the body. (In <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, the ventral aorta has divided in two; one half forms the <a href="/wiki/Ascending_aorta" title="Ascending aorta">ascending aorta</a>, while the other forms the pulmonary artery).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_133-4"><a href="#cite_note-VB-133">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the adult fish, the four chambers are not arranged in a straight row but instead form an S-shape, with the latter two chambers lying above the former two. This relatively simple pattern is found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartilaginous_fish" title="Cartilaginous fish">cartilaginous fish</a> and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ray-finned_fish" title="Ray-finned fish">ray-finned fish</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Teleost" title="Teleost">teleosts</a>, the conus arteriosus is very small and can more accurately be described as part of the aorta rather than of the heart proper. The conus arteriosus is not present in any <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a>, presumably having been absorbed into the ventricles over the course of evolution. Similarly, while the sinus venosus is present as a vestigial structure in some reptiles and birds, it is otherwise absorbed into the right atrium and is no longer distinguishable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_133-5"><a href="#cite_note-VB-133">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="656" data-file-width="656" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png/220px-Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png/330px-Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png/440px-Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tube-like heart (green) of the mosquito <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles_gambiae" title="Anopheles gambiae">Anopheles gambiae</a></i> extends horizontally across the body, interlinked with the diamond-shaped <a href="/wiki/Insect_wing#Muscles" title="Insect wing">wing muscles</a> (also green) and surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardial cells</a> (red). Blue depicts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_nuclei" title="Cell nuclei">cell nuclei</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:201px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthropod_body_struct_01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="61" data-file-width="199" decoding="async" height="61" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Arthropod_body_struct_01.png" width="199"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthropod_body_struct_01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Basic arthropod body structure – heart shown in red</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">Arthropods</a> and most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a> have an open circulatory system. In this system, deoxygenated blood collects around the heart in cavities (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sinus" title="wikt:sinus">sinuses</a>). This blood slowly permeates the heart through many small one-way channels. The heart then pumps the blood into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocoel" title="Hemocoel">hemocoel</a>, a cavity between the organs. The heart in arthropods is typically a muscular tube that runs the length of the body, under the back and from the base of the head. Instead of blood the circulatory fluid is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemolymph" title="Haemolymph">haemolymph</a> which carries the most commonly used <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_pigment" title="Respiratory pigment">respiratory pigment</a>, copper-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemocyanin" title="Haemocyanin">haemocyanin</a> as the oxygen transporter. Haemoglobin is only used by a few arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SOLOMON2010_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-SOLOMON2010-145">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some other invertebrates such as <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>, the circulatory system is not used to transport oxygen and so is much reduced, having no veins or arteries and consisting of two connected tubes. Oxygen travels by diffusion and there are five small muscular vessels that connect these vessels that contract at the front of the animals that can be thought of as "hearts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SOLOMON2010_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-SOLOMON2010-145">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">Squids and other cephalopods</a> have two "gill hearts" also known as <a href="/wiki/Branchial_heart" title="Branchial heart">branchial hearts</a>, and one "systemic heart". The branchial hearts have two atria and one ventricle each, and pump to the <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a>, whereas the systemic heart pumps to the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prosser1991_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prosser1991-147">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>Only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordates" title="Chordates">chordates</a> (including vertebrates) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemichordates" title="Hemichordates">hemichordates</a> have a central "heart", which is a vesicle formed from the thickening of the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> and contracts to pump blood. This suggests a presence of it in the last <a href="/wiki/Deuterostome" title="Deuterostome">common ancestor of these groups</a> (may have been lost in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echinoderms" title="Echinoderms">echinoderms</a>).
</p>

<div class="thumb" style="width: 240px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animated_Heart.gif" title="Animated Heart 3d Model Rendered in Computer"><img alt="Animated Heart 3d Model Rendered in Computer" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Animated_Heart.gif/160px-Animated_Heart.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Animated_Heart.gif/240px-Animated_Heart.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Animated_Heart.gif/320px-Animated_Heart.gif 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>

</p>

</div></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-alpha reflist-columns-2">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From the heart to the body</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Arteries that contain deoxygenated blood, from the heart to the lungs</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Supplying blood to the heart itself</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From the body to the heart</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Veins containing oxygenated blood from the lungs to the heart</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Veins that drain blood from the cardiac tissue itself</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note the muscles do <b>not</b> cause the valves to open. The pressure difference between the blood in the atria and the ventricles does this.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Depolarisation of the ventricles occurs concurrently, but is not significant enough to be detected on an ECG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson's2010528–530-83">[76]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
